# The 33 Laws of Typography

> Typography, done right, can enrich any message, making it beautiful and easy to understand. In this course, Jill Butler distills her many years of teaching and design consulting into 33 laws of typography. These laws are the details that designers can overlook or, in some cases, never learned, but that make for stronger compositions. Here these rules are broken down into five categories: documents

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography) | 2h 53m | Beginner | 79K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

- **2. 1. How to Format a Document** (6 videos)

- **4. 3. How to Format Smaller Blocks of Text** (10 videos)
- **5. 4. How to Use Punctuation Properly** (5 videos)
- **6. 5. How to Choose Typefaces** (3 videos)
- **7. Conclusion** (1 videos)

>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=10)** One of my favorite parts about design is taking long, really boring looking spans of text and turning these spans of text into beautiful, easy to read documents and it's really fun and it's pretty exciting because it doesn't take much.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=27)** You just need to follow the laws of typography.

>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=34)** It's just that a lot of people are not familiar with them.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=39)** Typography is just one part of a document and just one part of document design.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=44)** But it's a really important part that often gets overlooked or even ignored.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=50)** Steve Jobs who was the founder of Apple Computer studied calligraphy at Reed College and then went on to change the world forever with the invention of the Macintosh Computer.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=62)** One of the Macintosh computer's biggest innovations was the ability to create beautiful typography.

>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=82)** But because of Steve Jobs, all of us can create beautiful documents with gorgeous typography and that's huge.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=90)** It's revolutionary and this ability for all of us to create beautiful documents simply did not exist before 1984.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=102)** You may create documents as your primary job function or maybe as part of your larger job duties.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=108)** You may create documents as a hobbyist, or as a volunteer, or maybe as a student.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=113)** You may be creating resumes, web sites, e-books, presentations, student assignments, business cards, posters, brochures.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=123)** But no matter what type of documents that you're creating, they'll be more effective when the typography is both professional and beautiful.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=133)** Documents with professional, beautiful typography are more legible and that simply means that they're easier to read and that makes them better at communicating, which is the whole purpose of creating a document in the first place.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=148)** Documents with professional, beautiful typography are also more aesthetically pleasing.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=153)** They look better and because of this, these documents with beautiful typography, they create a good first impression and they set a positive tone for your audience before a single word is ever spoken or read.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=169)** This course is going to introduce you to the foundations of beautiful typography by explaining the 33 laws of typography and these 33 laws have been broken down into five categories.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=182)** How to format a document, how to format large bodies of text, how to format smaller blocks of text, how to use punctuation properly, and how to choose typefaces.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=195)** So if you are ready to start creating more effective documents with beautiful typography, then you're in the right place.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/introduction?u=76281980&t=203)** I really hope you enjoy this course and come to appreciate good typography as much as I do.

### 2. 1. How to Format a Document

#### 01 Distrust default software settings
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=0)** Law one: distrust default software settings.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=4)** When you start a new document, the software has some built in defaults that it's going to use for formatting your document.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=11)** And a lot of these settings have to do with typographic choices that you would make, like the typeface you'll be using.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=19)** The type size.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=21)** Line spacing.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=23)** Margin sizes.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=26)** And text alignment.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=28)** And these defaults exist because the software has to start somewhere.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=32)** But your job is to change all of these defaults.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=36)** You wanna change these settings and in fact, most, if not all of these default settings should be changed for every document you create.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=46)** So here are two business cards that both contain the same information.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=49)** This first card was created using software defaults, but on the second card, those defaults were changed and typographic decisions were made by the card's designer.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=61)** Now this first card contains all the information we need, but it really doesn't tell us anything else about the company.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=67)** It lacks personality and it's really not that visually appealing or exciting to look at.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=72)** It's not very memorable.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=75)** On top of that, it fails to communicate anything about the style and essence of The Mad Hatter.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=83)** The second card does a much better job of telling us more about the company.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=87)** It contains all the same necessary text information, but here we're also getting some clues.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=93)** We're learning a little more about The Mad Hatter.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=96)** And we get the feeling that this company is fancy, it's a little upscale and it's slightly formal.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=103)** And we're getting a lot of these clues from the typographic choices that were made by the designer.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=110)** So let's examine each card piece by piece to learn more about these choices that were made and how they affect the overall feel of this card.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=119)** So first, the typeface.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=121)** On the first card, the typeface being used is called Minion Pro, and that's the default typeface in the software that we used to create the card.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=130)** And on the second card, two different typefaces are being used, and these specific typefaces were chosen to create a more sophisticated tone.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=139)** And when the typefaces that you choose match the intended tone of the content, your message is going to be more memorable, and it's also going to be much more powerful.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=150)** So let's look at the type size.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=153)** On the first card, all of the text is the exact same size.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=156)** It's all 12 points, the default type size.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=159)** But on the second card, you can see that there's a few different sizes being used.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=164)** We've got large text, then we've got smaller text, and then we've got even smaller text.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=169)** And this mix of type sizes is helping to provide a visual hierarchy, and what a visual hierarchy is going to do for your documents is create a start point and an end point.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=179)** A visual hierarchy tells readers where they should begin in your document, and it provides a flow through that document.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=186)** It tells readers where they should focus their attention.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=191)** Now let's look at text alignment.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=193)** The first card is using all flush left, ragged right type, which is the default text alignment.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=200)** But on the second card, that default alignment has been changed, and all the text on this card is horizontally centered.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=206)** And what a horizontally centered alignment does, is it's creating a more balanced business card, and it's also adding to the formal, fancy tone.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=217)** Now line spacing.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=219)** On the first card, all the space between each line of text is the exact same.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=224)** It's all 2.4 points, which is the default line spacing.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=229)** But on the second card, the line spacing's been customized and it's been changed.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=233)** You can see the line spacing is bigger in some places and it's smaller in other places.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=238)** What this line spacing does, or what these changes in line spacing do is they provide a visual rhythm, and that's helping to chunk information, it's also providing pauses between these chunks.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=253)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=255)** You're going to want to replace default software settings with appropriate typographic settings, and when you do this, your documents are going to be more aesthetically pleasing, more interesting, and more memorable.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=268)** When you can align these settings and these choices to the personality and tone of the content, you're going to achieve maximum effect.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=275)** Your messages will be more powerful, more memorable, and therefore, more effective.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/01-distrust-default-software-settings?u=76281980&t=284)** That's law one: distrust default software settings.

>

> **Definitions:** is called (1)

#### 02 Ensure good contrast between text and background
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 2.
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=1)** Ensure good contrast between text and background.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=6)** When you're creating documents that are meant to be read, you want to make sure that the text is legible.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=11)** And all that means, legible text is clear and easy to read.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=16)** So a really important aspect of text legibility, is the amount of contrast between the text and background.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=24)** And so what exactly does contrast mean?
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=27)** Contrast is simply the difference between two colors.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=30)** And colors can be different in a lot of different ways.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=34)** We're going to focus on differences in lightness and darkness.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=38)** Because these differences have the biggest effect on text legibility.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=43)** Here's an example of high contrast between text and background.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=46)** And here we've got black text on a white background.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=50)** Black and white provide the highest possible contrast.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=53)** And that's because white is the lightest color, and black is the darkest color.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=58)** So this high contrast makes it really easy to see the difference between the text and background.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=64)** And that helps make this text easy to read.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=69)** So here's an opposite example.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=70)** Here's an example of low contrast between text and background.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=73)** Here we're using two different shades of grey, but the shades are really similar.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=79)** And this similarity makes it really difficult to see the difference between the text and background.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=84)** There's low contrast here.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=86)** The low contrast makes the text very difficult to read and this can cause eye strain and fatigue.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=93)** If you're using color on your designs, the guidelines are basically the same.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=97)** Colors themselves don't affect text legibility.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=100)** But what's important is the contrast between the colors or the difference between the lightness and the darkness of each color.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=108)** Here's an example of high color contrast between the text and background. And here we've got dark blue text, on a light yellow background.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=116)** And here's an example of low color contrast between text and background.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=120)** And just like in our grey scale example, this low contrast is going to make this text pretty difficult to read.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=126)** While the high contrast example would be just fine.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=130)** If you're designing with light text on a dark background, this is called Reverse Type.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=136)** And when there's a dark visually-heavy background, a lot of times small thin strokes can get lost.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=144)** And this is going to make your text harder to read.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=147)** So Reverse Type is fine for small chunks of text.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=150)** You could use it for things like pull quotes and short headlines when you're trying to call attention to something.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=155)** But you should really avoid Reverse Type for large amounts of text.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=158)** And when you're using Reverse Type it's best to stick with typefaces that don't contain really thin strokes.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=164)** So for this reason, Sans Serif typefaces tend to work pretty well.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=169)** Sans Serif typefaces lack Serifs.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=171)** Serifs are often really small and sometimes very thin and they can get easily swallowed up by your dark background.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=178)** Adding some letter spacing can also help with legibility when you're working with Reverse Type.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=184)** And this extra spacing helps keep the dark background from swallowing up the lighter text.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=189)** And if you look in this example you can see that there's some extra horizontal space between each of these characters.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=195)** And that's Letterspacing.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=197)** Some software programs also call this "tracking."
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=202)** So far, we've looked at a bunch of examples but they've all been solid text on solid backgrounds.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=208)** Legibility can become even more problematic when you're dealing with pattern backgrounds or photographic backgrounds.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=216)** It can be challenging to find the text color that provides high contrast with all the different parts of a complex background.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=223)** You really want to avoid these complicated backgrounds, unless you're going to make sure that you provide the necessary contrast and make the text legible.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=232)** So here's some text that we've overlaid on photograph.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=235)** And this text is pretty difficult to read, even though it's just a small chunk.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=240)** And that difficulty is because there's a lack of contrast between the text and all the different colors that appear in this complicated photographic background.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=250)** So now, we've added a dark rectangle behind the text.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=252)** And you can see that that's helped providing plenty of contrast between the text and all the different colors that are found in the background.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=260)** And that's really helping this text stand out, and make that text much more legible.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=267)** Remember that if you're creating a presentation that you're going to be projecting onto a wall, the color of that wall will also affect your presentation.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=275)** And depending on what type of wall you're projecting on, that wall can become a complicated background.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=281)** So you want to avoid projecting on to walls that have a lot of patters, textures, wallpaper.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=287)** You want to try to project onto light colored solid walls where there're no patters and no textures.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=295)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=298)** You want to make your documents as legible as possible.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=301)** And one way you can assure this is true, is by making sure that there's high contrast between text and its background.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=308)** If you're creating text-heavy documents, dark text on a solid light background is easiest to read.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=317)** That's Law 2.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/02-ensure-good-contrast-between-text-and-background?u=76281980&t=319)** Ensure good contrast between text and background.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (8), find (1)
> **Code Keywords:** type. (2), let (1)
> **Definitions:** is called (1)
> **Analogies:** just like (1)
> **Best Practices:** it's best to (1)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 03 Avoid chart junk and page junk
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 3, Avoid Chartjunk and Pagejunk.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=4)** These may be new terms for you, so let's start with a definition.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=8)** Chartjunk is the excessive and unnecessary use of graphical elements in charts and graphs.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=15)** So, let's look at an example to better understand this concept.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=19)** Here's a chart that is loaded with chartjunk, and this chartjunk is showing up as thick dark rules, darkly shaded background, all these pattern fills, and the large heavy text that's been used.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=40)** Now, here's the same chart with the same information but without all the chartjunk, and you can see in this example that everything in this chart is adding or clarifying meaning, and that's what you want when you're creating charts and graphs.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=53)** If you have elements that are not adding or clarifying meaning, get rid of them.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=58)** Now, for the elements that are adding meaning, reduce them down to their simplest form.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=64)** So, that means if you have borders that can be thinner, make them thinner.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=70)** If you have shading that can be lighter, make it lighter.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=75)** Keep reducing the graphic elements down until they're as simple as they can be but they're still visible and still meaningful.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=84)** You also want to watch out for pagejunk, and pagejunk is the excessive and unnecessary use of graphical elements on pages.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=94)** Pagejunk often shows up as boxes, shadows, and rules.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=101)** So, here's an example of a flyer that's suffering pretty badly from pagejunk, and you can see there's so many unnecessary graphical elements on this page.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=110)** The result is pretty unprofessional looking.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=113)** This page is very crowded.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=115)** It's a very distracting design.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=117)** There's so much going on that it's hard to know where to look first.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=122)** So, here's the same flyer, and it's been redesigned with no pagejunk.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=127)** You can see that overall the effect is more professional.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=130)** The page looks lighter.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=132)** It's more organized.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=134)** It's more pleasing to look at.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=136)** And so, all of these things added together equal that this page is more effective at communicating its message.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=144)** Now, it's not that you shouldn't ever use boxes and rules and shadows.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=149)** Boxes and rules can be a great way to add visual interest.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=152)** You can use them to highlight elements.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=154)** They can group elements, but it can be easy to go overboard when you're using these things, and so, you want to minimize the number of different elements on a page, and you want to keep the design of your boxes and rules pretty simple.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=170)** You want to watch out for things like double rules, fancy rules, overly thick rules.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=178)** All of these things look clunky.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=180)** They look very heavy, and they will junk up your page very quickly, and this is what's going to create pagejunk in your documents.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=189)** Remember that when you're using boxes and rules, text needs room to breathe.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=195)** Here's an example of text that's been crowded inside a box, and it looks like this text is just stuffed in there.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=200)** There's not enough margin.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=202)** Overall, this looks unprofessional.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=203)** It looks very very crowded and tight.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=206)** So, here's the same text, still inside a box, but now there's plenty of space for this text to breathe.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=211)** The margins are better.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=213)** It's lighter, and the effect is much more professional overall.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=219)** If you're using rules, the same guidelines would apply.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=223)** Here's some ruled text, and there's really not enough space between this text and the rule below it.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=228)** You can see that that rule is pushed up pretty tight against the bottom of that text.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=232)** And here's the same text, still with the rule, but now there's plenty of space for that text to breathe.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=240)** Keep in mind that the borders of your document also form a box and that your content has to live inside this box, and so, just like with the boxes and rules, you want to pad your content with an appropriate amount of white space.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=253)** You don't want to jam your content into this box.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=258)** So, here's an example where that's exactly what's happened.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=260)** This content has been crowded onto this page.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=263)** The margins are too small.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=264)** The content doesn't have room to breathe.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=266)** There's so much content and so little white space that it's creating a pretty uninviting page, and this page looks like a big heavy gray block.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=277)** Now, here's the same page, and it's been redesigned with an appropriate amount of white space, and you can see here that the white space is really being used as a design element.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=286)** White space, when it's used properly, is so much more than leftover space on the page.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=291)** White space adds professionalism to your documents.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=293)** It adds sophistication, and it gives your readers a visual break.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=299)** White space keeps your pages from being text-heavy gray blocks, and it makes your documents a lot more approachable and inviting.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=308)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=310)** Every element on your page or in your chart or graph should add or clarify meaning.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=316)** If an element's not adding or clarifying meaning, then it's junking up your document.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=321)** It's creating pagejunk or chartjunk, and this is distracting readers from your message.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/03-avoid-chart-junk-and-page-junk?u=76281980&t=330)** That's Law 3, Avoid Chartjunk and Pagejunk.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (3), break. (1)
> **Warnings:** watch out (2), keep in mind (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Analogies:** just like (1)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 04 Enforce a consistent style within a document
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law four, Enforce Consistent Style Within a document.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=5)** When you're designing documents you want to maintain visual consistency between similar elements.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=11)** And some of these elements that may appear in your document would include chapter titles, headings sub-headings, body text, image captions, page headers, and page footers.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=27)** You want to pick a typographic style for each of these elements and then enforce that style throughout your document.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=34)** Your goal is for all similar elements to be formatted consistently.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=38)** So this means that all body text should be formatted to look the same.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=42)** All captions should be formatted to look the same.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=45)** All headings, sub-headings, page headers, and page footers should all be formatted consistently throughout your document.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=55)** So lets take a look at what happens when this isn't true.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=58)** This is a page where consistent styles have not been applied.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=62)** And you can see if we take a look at the image captions for instance, in this caption, the text is italicized and it's right aligned.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=71)** But in this image caption the text is not italicized and the text here is center aligned.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=76)** These image captions are not even set in the same typeface, this caption is farther away from the image then this caption.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=85)** And the same inconsistencies apply to the chapter titles.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=89)** Each chapter title is actually different then the others.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=93)** This chapter title is 14 point bold text with a one point rule.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=98)** But this chapter title is 12 point text with a thicker three point rule.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=102)** Some of the chapter titles are bold, others are not bold, some chapter titles are all upper case, others are not.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=110)** And even in the body text if we look at the final paragraph there's extra space between each of these lines of text and it's different from the rest of the body text.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=120)** That means that this last paragraph has more leading than the others.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=124)** Overall the design of this document is pretty confusing.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=128)** When your readers are comparing different elements it's difficult to know what each one of them is.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=134)** If we look at the image captions, is this an image caption or is this a different type of caption.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=140)** Is it something else entirely.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=143)** The title for chapter two looks very different from the title for chapter three.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=147)** It's hard to know if that's on purpose and if so, why would that be.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=152)** The visual design of this document creates questions and confusion.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=156)** It distracts from the message.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=159)** And that's exactly the opposite of what we want to do when we're creating documents.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=164)** The result is an unorganized and ineffective document.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=169)** So here's the same page with the same content that's been redesigned and this time consistent formatting has been applied throughout.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=178)** You can see now each chapter title is formatted using a consistent style.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=183)** The same is true for body text and the image captions.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=187)** So now the result is a document that looks professional and clean.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=191)** There are no distractions from the message here.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=195)** Its easy for your readers to quickly scan this page and know which elements are related to others.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=201)** This visual consistency allows readers to focus on the content.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=206)** It also gives the document a professional appearance.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=211)** So before you begin creating a document it's important to do some upfront preplanning and decide what elements your document will contain and then make decisions about how to format each of these elements.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=225)** You're going to want to make decisions like what typeface you'll be using, the type sizes, text alignment, and leading.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=235)** If you do this up front you're going to save yourself a lot of time, especially if you create large multi-page documents.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=243)** Now the good news is that most software programs will allow you to set styles for different text elements.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=248)** And using these styles is a really great way to enforce consistency in a document and also automate this process for yourself.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=256)** Make it a lot easier.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=258)** So if you learn how to use these styles you're going to save yourself time and you're going to avoid headaches.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=264)** And a really nice thing is that if you do use styles and then you change your mind about a particular element, like if you want to change the type style of all your headings, you can go in and modify this style one time and it updates throughout your document.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=279)** If you're designing a website, you're going to want to use css or Cascading Style Sheets and that will accomplish the same thing.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=290)** Let's summarize, enforcing consistent style throughout your documents allows readers to focus on the content.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=298)** This means that your documents are going to be more effective, they're also going to look a lot more professional.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=305)** Enforcing consistent style helps visually group related elements and this makes it obvious to readers which elements are similar to others in your documents.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/04-enforce-a-consistent-style-within-a-document?u=76281980&t=315)** That's law four, Enforce Consistent Style Within a Document.

#### 05 Maintain a visual hierarchy
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law five, Maintain a Visual Hierarchy.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=5)** Maintaining a visual hierarchy is going to make your documents more professional, and it will make your messages more memorable.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=12)** A visual hierarchy directs the flow of a document and it does this by making the document structure clear to your readers.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=21)** So, a document structure may include things like document titles, chapter titles, headings, subheadings, body text, page headers, and page footers.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=34)** And so, your job is to format each of these elements in a way that makes this structure visible.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=41)** Now, a document structure is hierarchical, and that means that some elements are subordinate to other elements.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=48)** Your goal is to reveal this hierarchy and each element's place in it.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=54)** And there are a lot of different ways you can indicate visual hierarchy.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=57)** You can use bold, italics, type size, type face, type weight, white space, rules, boxes, leading, letter spacing, or uppercase characters.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=75)** So, let's look at a typical document hierarchy.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=78)** At the top level, we've got the document title.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=81)** Then there are level one headings, which are subordinate to the document title.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=85)** Level two headings are subordinate to level one headings.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=89)** Body text is subordinate to all the headings and all the subheadings.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=93)** Image captions are subordinate to the body text.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=97)** And document headers and footers are subordinate to the image captions.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=101)** So, the visual attributes of each element should correspond to that element's place in the document hierarchy.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=108)** That means each subordinate element should be formatted to look subordinate.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=114)** This will help readers know where to begin in your document and also how to understand your document's flow.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=122)** A visual hierarchy is going to add variety to your documents, it's going to add visual interest as well.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=128)** The visual hierarchy keeps your pages from looking too grey and too monolithic.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=135)** Here's a page that lacks a visual hierarchy and you can see that all the text on this page is set at the same type size and the same type face, with the exact same amount of emphasis.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=146)** There are no visual cues that provide any information about the difference between the title, headings, and body text.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=154)** And because everything on this page looks so similar, there's no obvious starting point, and overall, the result is a very grey, very dull page.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=165)** There's really no visual interest to attract readers.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=168)** There's nothing to really tell them where to begin, and there's not much that's going to keep their interest.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=175)** So here's a page with a different problem.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=177)** The elements on this page don't maintain a visual hierarchy.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=181)** When we look at this document, it's pretty difficult to get a quick grasp of each element in the document, and it's relationship to all the other elements.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=190)** So, if we look at it, the image caption, page header, and page footer are all larger than the body text.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=196)** The subheadings are larger than the book title.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=200)** The chapter title is the same size, and it's also the same weight as the body text.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=206)** Look at the words "Chapter Five", these are the largest words on the page.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=210)** So ask yourself, are these really the most important words on this page?
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=214)** They're probably not, but the way they're formatted they call the most attention to themselves, and they certainly look like the most important words on the page.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=223)** Overall, this visual formatting is pretty confusing.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=226)** It draws attention away from the content of the document, and that's always going to be a bad thing.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=234)** Now, here's a page where a visual hierarchy has been established, and it's also been maintained throughout the document.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=240)** And this visual hierarchy provides a very clear, professional, organized framework.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=246)** Even if you couldn't read this content, it's clear that this is the document title because it's the largest text on the page, and it's bold.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=254)** You can see that the level one heading is also bold but it's smaller than the document title because it's subordinate.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=260)** The words "Chapter Five" are smaller than the level one heading.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=263)** They're also not bold because they're not as important as the name of the chapter.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=268)** The subheadings are bold but they're smaller than the level one heading, they're also italicized to make them a little different.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=275)** The body text is smaller than all the headings and the image caption is smaller and is lighter than the body text.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=283)** The page header and page footer are the smallest elements on this page.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=287)** So, here a clear hierarchy has been established, and it's been maintained throughout the page.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=294)** The visual design of each page element reinforces this hierarchy.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=300)** So, before you begin creating a document, you're gonna want to do some preplanning and decide what elements your document will contain and then you'll be ready to make some design decisions about how you want to format each element so that a clear visual hierarchy is created and maintained.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=315)** You can use your software's built-in style sheets to help set up and apply and maintain this consistent hierarchy throughout your document.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=324)** And if you're a web designer, using Cascading Style Sheets, or CSS, is going to help provide the same framework.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=332)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=334)** Maintaining a visual hierarchy is important so that a document structure is quickly clear to readers.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=340)** A visual hierarchy gives readers an obvious starting point, and it directs the flow of a document.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=348)** Using a visual hierarchy is going to make your documents more professional, more organized, more engaging, and more effective.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/05-maintain-a-visual-hierarchy?u=76281980&t=359)** That's law five, Maintain a Visual Hierarchy.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Definitions:** means that (1), is a  (1)
> **Prerequisites:** before you begin (1), set up (1)

#### 06 Group related page elements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law six, Group Related Page Elements.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=5)** In most documents you're going to have some elements that are related to others in the same document.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=10)** For example, headings are related to sub-headings.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=14)** Sub-headings are related to paragraphs.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=17)** And captions are related to images.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=20)** So when you look at a document, these relationships should be obvious.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=24)** Related elements should be physically close to each other in order to form a visual group.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=31)** There's a Gesalt Principle that's called the Law of Proximity and this law states when people perceive an assortment of objects, they perceive objects that are close to each other as forming a group.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=43)** Let's see how we can apply this Law of Proximity to our document design and topography.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=52)** Here is an example of a page with some sub-headings and you can see that the sub-headings are equally distant from the text above them and the text below them.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=61)** This equal distance actually makes it look like these sub-headings are floating in space.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=67)** They're really not visually anchored to anything on the page and it's not clear that they're related to anything on the page.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=73)** They look like standalone pieces of text.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=76)** But we know that sub-headings are not standalone pieces of text, we know that each of these sub-heads is like a little headline for the paragraph that follows it.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=87)** So let's see how we can fix this issue.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=91)** Here's the same page, but this time we've moved the sub-heads and we've moved them closer to the body text that they relate to.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=99)** You'll see that there's more space on top of these sub-headings then there is below them.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=104)** What this does is it makes the relationship between the sub-heads and the paragraphs that follow them much more obvious.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=110)** It's much more clear that these sub-headings are related to these paragraphs of body text.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=119)** Here are two examples of images with captions.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=122)** In the first example, there's quite a bit of white space on top of the caption.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=126)** In the second example, the captions been moved up and now it's closer to the image to which it refers.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=133)** So looking at the first example, the captions not visually tied to the image or anything else on the page.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=139)** The caption looks like it's a little standalone piece of text.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=143)** And the second example it's much more clear that the image and the caption below it are related because of their physical proximity.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=153)** Be aware that the opposite of this law is also true.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=156)** If object are not related to each other, they should be moved farther apart.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=163)** Here's an example of a page that contains headers and footers.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=167)** You an see that the headers and footers are really close to the body text on this page, and the page is getting very visually crowded.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=174)** When you first look at it, it's really hard to even see the difference between the body text and the headers and the footers because they're all so close together.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=183)** Now since the headers and footers are not directly related to the body text, they should be physically separated from it.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=190)** So now in this example you can see that we've done that.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=193)** The headers and footers have been moved closer to the margins of the document where they belong and the body text has been given some breathing room.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=200)** And this makes the relationship much more clear.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=205)** When you make these relationships obvious readers are able to quickly grasp the relationship between elements on your page and your documents are more effective at communicating.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=218)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=220)** Related page elements should take advantage of the Gesalt Law of Proximity.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=225)** This means that related elements should be physically closer together on a page and that unrelated page elements should be physically separated from each other.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=236)** This helps to define the relationship of these elements to each other.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=240)** It also helps form visual groupings in your documents that's going to make your documents easier to read and make them more effective at communicating.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/06-group-related-page-elements?u=76281980&t=253)** That's law six, Group Related Page Elements.
