### 3. 2. How to Format Large Bodies of Text

#### 07 Set printed body text from 9 to 11 points
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law Seven, Set Printed Body Text at Nine to 11 Points.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=6)** When you're creating a document that's meant to be read, you want to make sure that the text is legible, and legible text is simply text that is clear and easy to read.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=18)** An important aspect of legibility is type size, so choosing the right type size is an important part of document design.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=27)** First, let's talk about how type is measured, and we'll go over a few important terms that you may or may not be familiar with.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=35)** The x-Height of a typeface is the height of a lowercase x in that typeface.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=41)** X-Heights are going to change from one typeface to another.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=44)** You may have one typeface that has a really large x-Height and another typeface at the same size that has a smaller x-Height.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=53)** The baseline is the imaginary line that letters sit on.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=57)** It's sort of like the lines on a ruled sheet of paper.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=62)** Ascenders are the parts of lowercase letters that extend above the x-Height as seen in the lowercase h or a lowercase b.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=72)** Descenders are the parts of lowercase letters that extend below the baseline.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=77)** You can see some descenders here in this lowercase g, in this lowercase y.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=83)** If you're dealing with print documents and presentation software, type is measured in points, and you'll hear designers talk about type on a page or in a document, and you'll hear them talk about 11-point type or 12-point type, 48-point type, and there are 72 points in one inch, so on a printed page, 36-point type would be half an inch tall.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=106)** The way type is measured is it's measured from the top of the ascenders to the bottom of the descenders in a typeface.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=114)** Here we have some 36-point type, and here we have 36-point type, and you can see that one typeface set at 36 points can look really different from another typeface that's set at the same size, and this is because of the different x-Heights in these typefaces and also the different heights of the ascenders and descenders.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=137)** If you're a web designer, type size works a little differently.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=139)** Users can actually change the size of the text on a webpage in their browser by changing their browser settings.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=147)** Online type's also measured a little differently, and you can specify it in a few different ways.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=153)** You can specify type sizes in absolute terms like this or you can use relative terms like this.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=165)** It's important to properly size the body text in your documents because body text makes up the bulk of multipage documents.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=172)** It's the text that you're going to want people to be able to focus on and read.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=178)** If body text isn't legible, then your document is not legible.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=182)** In a print document, you want to size your body text at nine to 11 points for maximum legibility.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=189)** Body text that is smaller than nine points can be difficult to read for long passages, and body text larger than 11 points often looks pretty large on the page, and it appears heavy and clumsy.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=204)** If you're creating a document that's going to be printed, it's really important that you print that document to review the type sizes.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=211)** A lot of times text on the screen may look great, but when it's printed, I often find that it looks too big and needs to be made smaller.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=220)** For websites, body copy is usually sized at 12 pixels to 16 pixels.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=226)** Any specific needs that your audience may have would outweigh these conventions, so, for example, older and younger audiences usually benefit from larger-than-normal type sizes, so keep that in mind if you're designing for a specific audience.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=242)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=244)** To make your documents as legible as possible, choosing the right type size is a really important decision.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=250)** Type that's too large can look heavy on the page or on the screen, and type that's too small can be difficult and straining to read.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=259)** When you're choosing type sizes, it's really important to review your documents in the environment in which it's going to be finally used.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=266)** Print documents should be printed, and presentations should be projected on a wall.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=272)** Websites should be coded up and reviewed in a web browser.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/07-set-printed-body-text-from-9-to-11-points?u=76281980&t=277)** That's Law Seven, Set Printed Body Text at Nine to 11 Points.

#### 08 Set body text two to three alphabets wide
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law eight.
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=1)** Set body text two to three alphabets wide.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=6)** When you're creating a document that's meant to be read you wanna make sure that the text is legible.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=11)** And we've talked about this before but legible text is simply text that is clear and easy to read.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=17)** An important aspect of legibility is line length.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=21)** Now, line length refers to the length of a line of text.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=24)** It's the same thing as the width of a block of text.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=28)** It's important that you set your body text blocks at a width that's just right.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=32)** You don't want them to be too narrow and you don't want the width to be too wide.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=39)** Here's an example of body text where the line length is too narrow.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=42)** And because these lines of text are so short your readers are going to be constantly jumping from the end of one line of text to the beginning of the next.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=51)** And that can be a little tiring and a little distracting.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=55)** Narrow text blocks can also cause odd right margins if your text is set flush left.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=61)** And this jagged edge is pretty visually distracting.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=64)** It makes the paragraph look messy.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=69)** In this example both the right and left edges are flush because this text is justified.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=75)** Now, in order to force both sides of this text block to be flush the software is going to add white space or gaps between these words.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=84)** And this white space and these gaps can greatly disrupt the flow of the body text and actually make it more difficult to read.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=92)** You'll notice how in some blocks of text the white space between words will start to group together.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=98)** It'll start to form a continuous line of white space that actually flows through your paragraphs.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=103)** And because of this effect these gaps are called rivers.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=109)** In this example the line length is set too wide.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=112)** And this can make it difficult for readers eyes to follow this line of text because it's so long.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=118)** Especially when they're moving from the end of one line of text to the beginning of the next.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=127)** Now in this example the line length is just right.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=130)** You can see that the right edge of the text is smooth, it's fairly even but the text block isn't set so wide that it's difficult to read.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=139)** So for maximum legibility, the width of your text blocks should be two to three alphabets wide.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=145)** And a way to figure out how to do this is to set a block of text using the typeface and the type size that you intend on using for your body text.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=153)** And then count the number of characters including spaces.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=157)** The width of your text block should be 52 to 78 characters wide.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=162)** And that's about two to three alphabets worth of letters.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=166)** This is the ideal line length for body text, for your chosen typeface at your chosen type size.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=173)** So if your text size gets larger or smaller, or if you decide to choose a different typeface, you're going to wanna recalculate the optimal line length because it will change.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=185)** If you've chosen a more complicated, slightly fancy typeface or if you're using reverse type, you're gonna wanna use a shorter line length for maximum legibility.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=195)** So you'd wanna choose a width of two alphabets or even a little bit less as a good place to start.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=202)** If you're using justified text you're going to want a longer line length to reduce rivers.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=209)** So a width of three alphabets is a good place to start.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=215)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=217)** The width of your body text has a big effect on text legibility.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=221)** If your body text is set too wide or too narrow it can make the text more difficult to read.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=228)** Narrow text blocks also have issues that can make them less visually attractive.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=234)** So for maximum legibility set your body text at two to three alphabets wide.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=240)** This is an easy way to obtain optimal line length.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=246)** That's law eight.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/08-set-body-text-two-to-three-alphabets-wide?u=76281980&t=248)** Set body text two to three alphabets wide.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (5)
> **Code Keywords:** type, (1), let (1)
> **Definitions:** refers to (1), is an  (1)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 09 Favor flush-left, ragged-right body text
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Voiceover] Law 9: Favor flush left, ragged right body text.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=6)** Text alignment is one of the choices that you're going to need to make when you're formatting body text.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=11)** Because body text makes up the bulk of multi-page documents, the type of alignment you choose has a big effect on the document as a whole.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=21)** The most common alignments used for body text are flush left and justified.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=27)** This is flush left, ragged right text.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=30)** In flush left text, you can see that the left edge of the text forms a straight line and the right edge is ragged.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=37)** This means that the right edge is allowed to be uneven.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=41)** The shape of the right edge of this text block is determined by where the words end and the lines naturally break.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=49)** Now this is justified text.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=51)** Justified text is text that has both flush left and flush right edges.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=57)** You can see in this block of text, the left edge forms a straight line and the right edge also forms a straight line.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=66)** So when text is set justified, in order to force the right edge to form a straight line, the software is going to add extra space between words.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=75)** Depending on the software you're using, justifying text can cause large gaps of white space between words.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=83)** When these horizontal gaps between the words are actually larger than the vertical gaps between the lines of text, the horizontal gaps will visually merge together.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=93)** And they're going to appear to flow through your paragraphs or your blocks of text.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=98)** When that happens, these horizontal gaps have formed rivers.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=103)** Rivers call a lot of attention to themselves, and they pull attention away from the text in your document, and that's always a bad thing.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=111)** You want readers to focus on the content in a document, not on distractions caused by design decisions.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=120)** Shorter line lengths are going to cause larger, more frequent rivers.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=125)** So if you must justify body text, you need to make sure that your line length is long enough to minimize rivers.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=131)** A line length of 3 alphabets or 78 characters is a good place to start.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=139)** The best solution is to use flush left, ragged right alignment for body text.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=144)** Flush left text is a more natural, modern convention, and it will make your documents look more modern and less rigid.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=153)** In flush left alignments, all words have equal space between them, and so rivers are not a problem.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=160)** Let's look at some other text alignments and talk about why you might use them.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=165)** This is flush right, ragged left text.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=168)** In flush right text, you can see that the right edge of the text forms a straight line and the left edge is ragged.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=175)** In documents, you'll see flush right alignment used to format elements like figure captions, author attributions, quotes, maybe tabular data.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=187)** Languages that are read right to left, like Arabic and Hebrew, are set flush right.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=193)** Flush right alignments are also used in single page documents like posters, invitations, maybe business cards, to group elements and add some visual excitement or interest to the page.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=207)** Another alignment option is centered text.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=210)** When text is horizontally centered, there's a strong center axis and both the right and left edges are ragged.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=218)** Center text is often used for document titles, formal invitations, and other documents where a more stable, conservative mood is desired.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=230)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=232)** Flush left, ragged right is the best choice for body text alignment.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=237)** Flush left text eliminates rivers and make your documents look more modern.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/09-favor-flush-left-ragged-right-body-text?u=76281980&t=243)** That's Law 9: favor flush left, ragged right body text.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (4)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), break. (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), means that (1)
> **Speakers:** - [voiceover] (1)

#### 10 Separate sentences with one space, not two
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 10: Separate sentences with one space, not two.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=6)** Many people were taught, and are still being taught, that when typing, two spaces should be added at the end of a sentence.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=13)** This is a convention that exists because of outdated technology.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=18)** It was never meant to be applied to modern professionally produced documents.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=23)** Here's a paragraph that is set with two spaces between each sentence.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=28)** And this paragraph is set with one space between each sentence.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=33)** The point of a document is for readers to focus on the text, not the spaces between sentences.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=40)** In this first example, the extra spaces call way too much attention to themselves.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=45)** The amount of white space is jarring, and it's disrupting the flow of the paragraph.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=51)** In the second example, the spaces between sentences don't get in the way and overall, the paragraph is much more visually pleasing.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=61)** If you open a modern professionally typeset book, magazine, brochure, or other printed document, you will see one space between all sentences.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=72)** So why do people use two spaces at the end of sentences?
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=76)** This convention exists because of the typewriter.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=80)** It's important to understand that typewriters and computers are very different machines, and each has unique capabilities and requirements.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=88)** One big difference between typewriters and computers is the typefaces that are available on each.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=94)** Typewriters have mechanical requirements that dictate the use of monospaced typefaces.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=100)** An example of a monospaced typeface that you may have heard of is Courier.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=105)** In contrast, almost all typefaces on modern computers are proportionally spaced typefaces.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=111)** So what does that mean?
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=113)** In a monospaced typeface, all characters are given the same amount of horizontal space.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=119)** But in a proportionally spaced typeface, each character is given a different amount of horizontal space depending on the character size and shape.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=128)** This makes sense, doesn't it?
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=130)** A lower case 'i' and an upper case 'W' are not the same width and shouldn't be given the same amount of horizontal space.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=139)** In a monospaced typeface, a space is as wide as every other character on the page.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=145)** It became a convention to use two spaces at the end of a sentence so that the break between sentences would be visually obvious.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=153)** In a proportionally spaced typeface, characters are all different widths, so one space is plenty to signal a break between sentences.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=163)** Two spaces at the end of a sentence are not needed and are incorrect when creating documents on a computer.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=169)** Now, you may have never used a typewriter, so why would you be following a typewriter convention?
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=176)** Probably because you were taught by someone who did use a typewriter and didn't know that the rules need to change when you're creating documents on a computer.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=186)** If you're a person who creates text content, stop hitting the space bar twice between sentences.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=193)** It's an old convention, and it's no longer needed.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=197)** If you receive content that was created by someone else, the first thing you should do with these documents is perform a quick search-and-replace.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=206)** Get rid of the double spaces, and replace them with single spaces.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=212)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=214)** Putting two spaces at the end of a sentence is an old convention that exists because of the typewriter.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=221)** These extra spaces are disrupting the visual flow of the text in your document and calling attention to themselves.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=229)** This is pulling attention away from the text in your document.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=234)** So get rid of those extra spaces.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=236)** It's an easy way to make your documents instantly more professional.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=243)** That's Law 10.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/10-separate-sentences-with-one-space-not-two?u=76281980&t=245)** Separate sentences with one space, not two.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** else, (1), let (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), is an  (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 11 Don't allow less than seven characters on a line
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=0)** Law 11: Don't allow less than seven characters on the line.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=7)** When you're formatting paragraphs of text, sometimes a single word, or maybe a short phrase, is going to end up as the last line of a paragraph.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=14)** It's pretty much inevitable.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=16)** Notice how this dangling word is causing visual unbalance in our paragraph.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=22)** Because our paragraph is not sitting on a visually stable base, it looks like it could even just tip right over.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=30)** And besides being visually unappealing, having just one word on this last line is also wasteful.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=37)** We've used an entire line for just our one little word.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=41)** And that's just not a good use of white space.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=43)** White space in your designs should be planned.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=47)** This is unplanned, wasted white space.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=52)** You may hear people talk about these short lines of text or you may read something about them, and sometimes they get referred to as 'widows,' sometimes they get referred to as 'orphans,' and if you start poking around, you'll see that there are a lot of different conflicting definitions and uses of these two terms.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=69)** There's not a lot of agreement out there on exactly what they mean and how they're used.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=75)** But for our purposes, it doesn't really matter.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=77)** It's not all that important to unravel this confusion.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=80)** You can call it whatever you want, just eliminate them.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=85)** A minimum of seven characters on a line of text is a good rule of thumb, and that's a pretty good place to start.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=93)** Keep in mind that our goal here is good visual balance while efficiently using the space on our page.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=99)** So the exact number of characters is not all that important as long as you're achieving this goal.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=106)** And so now you can see, we fixed the issue.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=109)** And in this example, we're using the same text as before, but the dangling final word is no longer there.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=116)** And we fixed it by widening our text block just a little bit.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=119)** And by doing that, by widening the text block, we caused the text of this paragraph to re-wrap, or re-flow, and now that final word jumped up to the previous lines.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=130)** So we no longer have our one little dangling word at the end of our paragraph.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=136)** Widening the text block is one way to fix this issue, but there are many other possible fixes as well.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=142)** And the solution that you choose is going to depend on how much flexibility you have in either changing your page design or possibly editing the text.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=154)** If you have the ability to edit the content, this is often the best choice for long blocks of text.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=160)** By adding or modifying or even deleting a word or two, you can usually fix the problem of dangling words.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=169)** If editing the text is not an option, and sometimes it just may not be, you're going to need to make some adjustments to the document design.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=177)** And these adjustments could include things like changing the size of the text to make it either a little bit larger or maybe a little bit smaller.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=188)** You can also force a word to wrap down to the next line of text, and you can do this by using what's called a 'soft return.'
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=195)** A soft return is created when you hold down the shift key and then hit the enter key on your keyboard.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=202)** A soft return is an invisible character and it signals the end of a line.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=207)** When you hit the enter key on your keyboard, that's called a 'hard return,' and that signals the end of a paragraph.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=214)** You can also insert a non-breaking space, and a non-breaking space is a special space character.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=221)** And what it does is, it forces words to stay together on the same line of text.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=226)** And so if you put a non-breaking space in between the last two words of your paragraph, those two words would always appear together on the same line.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=235)** They're not allowed to be broken at the space.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=239)** Non-breaking spaces are also available in HTML, if you're a web designer, and you access them by using this character entity.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=251)** This law applies not only to body text, but you also want to use this same law if you're dealing with document titles, subheadings, invitation text, et cetera.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=263)** You want to avoid having fewer than seven characters per line in all your documents.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=270)** Here's an example where we've got a title, and you can see that there's some dangling text here.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=276)** And then here's the same title, without the dangler.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=280)** We fixed this by making the size of the title just a little bit smaller, and it was small enough to force that last line to jump up to the previous line of text.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=290)** So it's no longer all by itself.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=293)** And you can see just by looking at this how much better it looks.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=297)** Now the text is visually balanced, the large gap is gone, and we're not wasting all of that white space.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=305)** So here's an example of a party invitation where there's some unbalanced, dangling text.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=311)** And then here's a better, visually balanced version.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=315)** Our invitation looks better, it's cleaner, and the dangling text is not getting in the way and calling so much attention to itself.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=325)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=326)** Always fix your dangling words and phrases.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=330)** These short lines of text are disrupting the balance in your documents and on your pages.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=335)** They're wasting space, and they're calling too much attention to themselves.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=342)** These dangling words and these dangling phrases are also disrupting the flow of text in your documents.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=348)** So fixing these dangling words is going to make your documents look more professional.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=353)** They'll look more visually appealing.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=356)** And you're going to be maximizing the use of space on your pages.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/11-don-t-allow-less-than-seven-characters-on-a-line?u=76281980&t=362)** That's Law 11: Don't allow less than seven characters on the line.
