#### 12 Avoid bad paragraph breaks
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 12, Avoid Bad Paragraph Breaks.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=6)** When you're creating a document that has a large amount of text, that text will most likely be broken into paragraphs, and these paragraphs are going to have to break across pages in your document.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=17)** When paragraphs do break across pages, it's important to make sure that they're breaking well, that they're breaking at good places.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=26)** In other words, avoid bad paragraph breaks.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=29)** What exactly does that mean?
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=33)** Here's an example of a bad paragraph break.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=37)** You can see that this line of text at the top of this page is actually the last line of text from the paragraph on the previous page.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=45)** When a paragraph contains multiple lines of text, you don't want to leave one line of text at the beginning of a page.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=53)** You don't want the paragraph to break that way.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=56)** You also don't want to leave one line of text at the end of a page, and here's an example of that type of bad break.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=64)** This line of text right here is actually the first line of a paragraph that continues on the next page.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=71)** Now, keep in mind that we're talking about paragraph breaks here, so we're talking about paragraphs that contain more than one line of text.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=79)** If you have paragraphs in your document that all fit on one line, then it's fine for those lines of text to appear at the beginning or the end of a page.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=88)** There's no paragraph break there.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=91)** In multi-line paragraphs, you want to keep at least two lines of text grouped together at all times.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=99)** Keeping at least two lines of text together forms a block or a group that carries more visual weight than just one little line of text.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=108)** When you allow your paragraphs to break across pages and leave these single lines of text at the beginning or the end of a page, you're not only creating visual unbalance, but you're also breaking up the point of the paragraph or the text in a paragraph in a way that makes it a little bit harder to read and a little bit more difficult for readers to stitch those thoughts back together while they're reading.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=134)** This is another type of bad break.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=137)** You don't want to leave a heading or a subheading by itself at the bottom of a page.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=142)** This heading goes with the paragraph that follows it, right?
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=146)** We know that they should be grouped together, but the paragraph starts on the next page for this heading, so by allowing our paragraph to break here, we've completely disrupted the flow of the document, and we've made it just a little bit harder for the reader to understand exactly what's going on.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=166)** Here, we fixed this break.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=169)** A subhead should always be followed by at least two lines of text.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=175)** There are lots of different ways that you can fix bad breaks, and there's really not a one-size-fits-all solution.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=183)** The solution you choose and the way you fix your bad breaks is really going to depend on the specific content you're working with and your ability to change either the page design or to edit the content.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=197)** If you can change the text or the content, or maybe if you have a close working relationship with the author or the editor, editing the text is often the easiest way to fix bad breaks.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=210)** Sometimes just by adding, deleting, or modifying a few words, you can make enough of a difference to fix your bad break issue.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=220)** If editing the text isn't an option, and sometimes it just won't be, you're going to need to make some adjustments to the document design, and these adjustments could include maybe using your software settings to change what are called the Keep Options.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=237)** Keep Options tell your software how many lines of text must be kept together at all times.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=245)** You can also change the width of a text box, and that's going to force the text to rewrap.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=251)** In other words, you're going to change the way that the text is laid out on the page, and that can fix your bad break issues.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=260)** You can also change the height of a text box.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=262)** You can make a text box just a little bit shorter or even a little bit taller, and that's also going to force your text to rewrap on the page, and that can fix your bad breaks.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=274)** If you have a lot of headings or subheadings in your document, just by tweaking the amount of vertical spacing that's surrounding these headings can be enough of a change.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=286)** You can either add a little bit of space or maybe delete a little bit of space.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=289)** That can make enough of a difference that you can fix your bad break issues that way often.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=296)** If you have any photographs or images or even a pull-quote, maybe a chart or a graph that goes with your content, just by adding an element like that to your page, you're going to force the text to rewrap.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=309)** You're going to force your text to reflow in your document, and that can be a way that you can fix your bad breaks.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=318)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=321)** Bad paragraph breaks disrupt the flow of your documents.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=326)** By avoiding bad paragraph breaks, you're making your documents look more professional, and you're making them more legible and more user-friendly.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/12-avoid-bad-paragraph-breaks?u=76281980&t=337)** That's Law 12, Avoid Bad Paragraph Breaks.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** break. (4), delete (1), let (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (5)
> **Definitions:** in other words (2)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 13 Avoid line-breaking hyphens
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 13.
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=1)** Avoid line-breaking hyphens.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=6)** Hyphens are useful little bits of punctuation.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=9)** And sometimes they're necessary to improve readability and help clarify meaning in your documents.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=15)** There are some words like the word twenty-two that require a hyphen.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=19)** And here the hyphen's being used to eliminate confusion and to help your readers get a quick grasp on exactly what the text is trying to say.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=29)** But using hyphens to line break words is not necessary and should be avoided.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=34)** So what does that mean?
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=36)** What are line-breaking hyphens?
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=39)** Line-breaking hyphens are hyphens that are automatically added by the software that you're using.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=45)** These hyphens are automatically inserted when an entire word won't fit at the end of a line of text.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=52)** The software is going to put the beginning of the word on the line, maybe a few characters, then the software adds a hyphen and then the software continues the rest of the word on the next line of the paragraph.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=65)** And I'm sure you've seen these a lot, you probably see them all the time.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=69)** But here's an example of a paragraph that contains line-breaking hyphens.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=74)** Hyphens that are used as line breaks are visually unappealing and noisy.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=80)** They also break the readers' concentration and reading rhythm.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=84)** You're asking readers to rebuild words in their mind when these words are being split across multiple lines of text.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=93)** So in short, line-breaking hyphens are ugly and disruptive and should be avoided.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=101)** So, here's that same paragraph, the same text we had before and this time the hyphenation has been turned off.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=108)** And notice how much cleaner this second example is.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=112)** The paragraph looks so much nicer.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=115)** It's also easier to read because words are not being broken across multiple lines of text.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=122)** There may be times when for some reason you have to use hyphens to line break words and if that's the case, make sure you use them correctly so that they stay out of the way and are as unobtrusive as possible.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=137)** Don't ever hyphenate titles, subheads or proper names.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=143)** Don't allow more than two hyphens consecutively in a paragraph.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=148)** We can probably best see this through an example.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=150)** So here's an example that contains more than two hyphens consecutively.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=156)** You can see that this paragraph is pretty visually cluttered and pretty noisy with all of these hyphens.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=164)** Don't hyphernate URLs or email addresses.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=169)** When hyphens appear in these special bits of text you're changing the meaning of the text and introducing errors.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=178)** Don't allow your software to make all the hyphenation decisions.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=182)** Modern desktop publishing software has built-in hyphenation control.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=186)** And you need to become familiar with where those controls are in the software that you use.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=193)** These hyphenation controls are where you can turn off hyphens completely.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=198)** If you have to use hyphens for some reason, this is also where you can specify the number of consecutive hyphens that can appear in a paragraph.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=208)** You can even tell the software how many letters should appear before and after hyphens by giving it a minimum.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=216)** If you're a web designer you can use css code to turn off automatic hyphenation on your webpages.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=224)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=226)** Line-breaking hyphens are visually unattractive and disrupt the flow of a sentence.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=232)** For these reasons they should be avoided.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/13-avoid-line-breaking-hyphens?u=76281980&t=236)** That's law 13, avoid line-breaking hyphens.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** require (1), case, (1), let (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 14 Signal new paragraphs once, not twice
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 14, Signal New Paragraphs Once, Not Twice.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=7)** You're probably aware that a paragraph is a collection of sentences that all relate to one topic.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=13)** Multi-page documents are broken into paragraphs to chunk the content and make it easier for your readers to digest.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=21)** Using paragraphs also helps break up really large blocks of text, it makes your documents more visibly interesting, and keeps them from looking like big gray blocks on a page.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=34)** So it's important that you give your readers visual cues or signals when one paragraph is ending and a new paragraph is beginning.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=44)** And you can signal new paragraphs in a couple of ways.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=47)** You can indent the first line of a new paragraph, or you can add some vertical space on top of the new paragraph.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=56)** You want to choose one or the other, don't use both these signals at the same time.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=63)** Indenting the first line of a new paragraph and adding white space on top of it is redundant.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=69)** You're oversignaling to your readers and your creating awkward, distracting white space in your documents.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=76)** So here's an example of a page with a few paragraphs on it.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=81)** Notice that each new paragraph is indented and that there's also white space above each new paragraph.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=89)** And here's the same text this time just with indents that are being used to signal each new paragraph.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=96)** And here again is the same text this time with vertical white space added at the top of each paragraph to signal a new paragraph.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=106)** Compare all three of these examples and notice how clunky this first example looks.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=111)** It's just too much, you told me your're going to start a new paragraph and then you told me again, I got it.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=118)** You don't have to tell me twice.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=120)** The white space that you're creating by this vertical gap plus the indent is awkward.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=126)** It's a little distracting, it's a weird shape.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=130)** The second two examples show how to correctly signal new paragraphs.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=134)** These pages look much more organized, they're neater, they're more professional, overall the page design and the white space is less distracting.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=146)** This problem happens quite a bit with paragraphs at the start of a new chapter or at the start of a document.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=153)** Here's an example of a paragraph at the start of a new chapter and you can see here the paragraph is indented.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=159)** And here's the same page this time without the indent.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=163)** The second page looks much better.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=167)** In the first there's too much extra unnecessary, awkwardly shaped white space.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=174)** As a reader I already know you're starting a new paragraph, you told me that when you put the chapter title at the top of the page and when you added some space below that title.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=184)** You don't need to tell me again.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=188)** If you're going to use an indent to signal a new paragraph, don't hit the space bar to create the indent.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=195)** The best way to create an indent is to use a first line indent in your software.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=202)** How big should a paragraph indent be?
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=205)** A paragraph indent should be about one to two times as wide as they type size that you're using.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=211)** This is going to give you an indent that's easily seen but not so big that it looks awkward.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=218)** If you're using 11 point body text the correct paragraph indent would be 11 to 22 points wide.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=227)** Now if you're going to add vertical white space instead to signal a new paragraph, don't create this space by hitting the return key twice, this results in a space that is unnecessarily large.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=239)** You want to use either the space before or the space after settings in your paragraph settings to set the exact amount of space that you want.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=248)** So what is that, how big should this space be?
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=252)** A vertical space equal to about 50 to 80 percent of the body text is the best size.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=258)** If you're using 10 point body text that means that the vertical space between paragraphs should be five to eight points tall.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=268)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=270)** When you want to signal the start of a new paragraph remember that you only need to use one signal.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=276)** Use either a first line indent or a vertical space, not both.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=282)** If a paragraph is at the beginning of a document or at the beginning of a new chapter there's no need to use an indent or add extra white space, the chapter title or the sub-heading is the signal.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=296)** That's law 14.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/14-signal-new-paragraphs-once-not-twice?u=76281980&t=298)** Signal New Paragraphs Once, Not Twice.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 15 Break up large blocks of text
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 15: Break up large blocks of text.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=6)** Assuming you're creating a document that is meant to be read, it's important that you make the text look inviting and legible.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=15)** It's the author and the editor's job to make sure that the text is interesing, that it's age-appropriate, and all of those other really important things.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=24)** But as a designer, it's your job to visually invite the reader into the page, and then keep their interest throughout the document.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=33)** Single color text on a single color background, repeated page after page, is a sure-fire way to create a boring looking document.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=42)** No matter how well written the text is, many people won't even give content a chance if the document looks boring and if the amount of text appears to be overwhelming.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=56)** Here's a page that's full of black text on a white background.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=60)** Imagine reading a 25-page document that looked like this, page after page.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=67)** The black text and the white background start to blur together and they create a big, gray, heavy, uninviting block on this page.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=77)** Here's the same text, and this time, it's been broken up with some typographic embellishments.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=82)** The dull grayness of this page is gone.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=86)** The page is much more inviting.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=88)** It's also much more aesthetically pleasing.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=92)** Our first example over here looks like a bunch of text was just dumped on the page without a whole lot of thought.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=99)** But in our second example, it looks like some thought actually went into making this document look inviting.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=107)** This page looks like it was designed.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=111)** There are a lot of different ways that you can break up large blocks of text, so let's talk about a few of them.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=120)** Drop caps or large initial caps.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=124)** A drop cap is a large capital letter that drops down into the body of the text.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=131)** A large initial cap is an initial character that's been set at a much larger size than the rest of the body text or the text on the page.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=139)** This initial cap shares the same baseline as the rest of the text.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=145)** Rules.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=147)** Rules can be professional, really simple ways to enhance a page.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=152)** Try adding rules to headers and footers, or even to some of the headings and titles on your page to help break up that text.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=161)** Pull quotes.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=163)** Pull quotes are short passages that have been pulled out of the document's text and highlighted.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=171)** Pull quotes work really well if there's a sentence or a quote that really sums up the point of the text on a page.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=179)** White space.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=181)** Adding larger margins to your pages is going to give the body text, or the text on the page, extra breathing room.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=189)** And that's going to help lighten up the feel of the page and get rid of that heavy, blocky feeling.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=195)** Graphics.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=197)** Photos, illustrations, maybe charts, graphs, are a great way to break up text on a page.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=204)** Images can be a wonderful way to add some pizzazz to a layout.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=209)** But keep in mind that they're not required.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=211)** If you don't have access to any great images that work with your content, or maybe if your content doesn't lend itself to any of these types of graphics, that's okay.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=222)** Keep in mind that all the examples we've looked at so far have achieved our objective of breaking up the page and making our documents more aesthetically pleasing.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=231)** They've achieved that objective without the use of any photos or any illustrations.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=236)** These graphics are great; they're a great thing to use.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=240)** But they're not required.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=243)** The point is to add enough enhancements to break up the text in your documents and then stop.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=251)** Don't add any more than what's needed.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=253)** You don't want to create a document that is full of page junk.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=258)** Here's an example where that's exactly what happened.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=262)** This page is over-embellished.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=265)** The page has become crowded.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=268)** It feels amateurish.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=270)** There's so much going on, it's hard to know where to look first.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=273)** It's hard for our reader to know where to devote their attention, where to start on the page.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=278)** Overall, the result is disjointed and pretty frantic.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=283)** Knowing when to stop is a really important part of typography and page design.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=289)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=292)** If you're working on a text-heavy multi-page document, it's especially important to break up the text and make the document more inviting and less intimidating.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=303)** This also makes your documents more visually pleasing and less boring.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/15-break-up-large-blocks-of-text?u=76281980&t=310)** That's law 15: Break up large blocks of text.

### 4. 3. How to Format Smaller Blocks of Text

#### 16 Emphasize ten percent or less of text
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 16: Emphasize 10 percent or less of text.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=7)** There will be occasions when you need to emphasize some text, to call attention to it.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=13)** Emphasizing text is a great way to add visual interest to your documents and break up large gray text blocks.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=22)** Emphasis also helps signal to readers that certain words require extra attention.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=27)** But you want to be careful.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=29)** You don't want to emphasize too much text on a page because then you've basically emphasized nothing.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=36)** Emphasis should be limited so that, when it is used, it does what it's meant to do, which is call attention.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=43)** You should emphasize 10 percent or less of visible text on a page.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=49)** This means that, for every 10 words, only one word should be emphasized.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=54)** Any more than that, and you risk over-emphasizing.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=59)** In these first couple of examples, we'll look at emphasizing body text.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=63)** In this first example, notice how most of the text in this paragraph is bold.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=69)** Bold text is a great way to emphasize text, but this is too much.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=75)** So much text here is bold that the bold loses its impact.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=79)** Now, in this second example, you can see that only a few key words are emphasized.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=85)** Notice how those few words really jump off the page and call your attention.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=92)** Here, the bold text is doing its job.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=95)** It's emphasizing the key words without overdoing it.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=101)** Now let's look at an example using a business card.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=104)** In this first card, all the text on the card is big and attention-grabbing.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=110)** Now, the problem is that because all of the text is basically the same size, nothing really stands out, even though it's all big text.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=120)** In this example, you can see that certain elements on this card have been emphasized using different text sizes.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=129)** Here, the person's name is the largest item on the card.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=133)** That helps emphasize it.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=134)** It helps the name stand out.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=137)** Then the job title is the next-largest size text on the card, and then contact information is the smallest text on the card.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=147)** On this poster, here we're emphasizing text using color.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=151)** We've decided to use red as our highlight color.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=155)** Because so much of the text is red, the red text is not functioning as a highlight.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=160)** If the goal was to use the color red to highlight text, this design is not achieving it.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=167)** In the second example, just a few bits of text have been colored red, and that helps them jump off the page.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=173)** The red highlights are now serving their purpose and they're emphasizing important information.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=182)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=184)** When you're creating a document, pay attention to any words or phrases that are particularly important to the document's message.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=191)** It's a great idea to emphasize this text and really help it stand out.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=196)** This signals to readers that emphasized text is especially important.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=201)** But you want to be careful and not over-emphasize.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=206)** By emphasizing everything on a page, emphasis becomes the norm and nothing stands out.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=213)** Nothing looks more important than anything else, and therefore, nothing is emphasized.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=221)** That's law 16.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/16-emphasize-ten-percent-or-less-of-text?u=76281980&t=223)** Emphasize 10 percent or less of text.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), require (1), else, (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), means that (1)
> **Warnings:** be careful (2)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 17 Avoid all caps and underlined text
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 17, avoid all caps and underlined text.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=7)** On a typewriter, there are really two ways to emphasize text and help call attention to it.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=13)** All caps and underlines.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=15)** That's fine if you're using a typewriter but if you're not, and you're probably not, it's time to abandon those bad habits and learn better ways, more modern ways to emphasize text.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=28)** In body text, text set in all caps is very clunky and very heavy on the page.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=35)** All caps letters look huge and they overwhelm the text that surrounds them.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=40)** If you're going to use all caps, limit their use to titles and sub-heads in text-heavy documents.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=46)** This will ensure that they're used sparingly.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=50)** For other documents like posters or invitations and business cards, it can be easier to incorporate text in all caps without it being so visually disruptive.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=61)** Underlines are a typewriter convention that told typesetters to set the underlined text in italics.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=69)** Professionally typeset documents do not use underlines.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=73)** If you want to add horizontal lines to headings or in pages headers or footers, use rules.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=81)** You have much more control over rules and you can style them to visually fit in your document.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=86)** The built-in underline formatting style should never be used, period.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=92)** On a computer, with desktop publishing software, we have many, many more options to emphasize text and all of these options are more professional, they're more visually pleasing, and they're the right way to emphasize text so let's look at some of those.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=108)** Bold.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=109)** Using bold text is a great way to emphasize text and help it stand out.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=115)** Italics.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=116)** Italicized text is also a great emphasis technique.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=120)** Italic text is more visually subtle than bold text and it can be a nice way to add some emphasis in a quiet, subdued way.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=130)** Be aware that some words need to be set italic in order to be correct.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=135)** These include things like the names of books, the names of magazines, movies, TV shows, works of art, albums, planes, ships and trains.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=146)** There are other words that also need to be italicized as well.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=149)** It's great to be familiar with these rules and apply them in your documents.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=155)** Small caps.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=157)** Small caps can be a great way to use capital letters for emphasis, without all that extra visual weight, that all caps contain.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=166)** Small caps are uppercase letters that are as tall as a lowercase x in a given typeface.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=176)** Using a different typeface.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=179)** If you're using a different typeface for emphasis, make sure it's different enough from the rest of your text to stand out.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=186)** If you're using this technique in body text, align x heights of both typefaces to help integrate them and make them look cohesive on the page.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=198)** Spacing.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=199)** Adding extra space around a block of text can be a very effective, unexpected way to emphasize a word or a phrase.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=207)** This technique doesn't work so well in body text but it can be very effective on business cards, posters, invitations and documents of that type.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=218)** Size.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=219)** Changing the size of a chunk of text and making it really big or really small can effectively call attention to it.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=226)** This is another technique that really doesn't always work so well in body text but it can be very effective in other types of documents.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=234)** Color.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=235)** Adding color is a great way to emphasize text.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=239)** But make sure that the emphasis color you choose is different enough from your text color to stand out.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=245)** Make sure it's also still providing enough contrast with your background to make it legible.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=250)** Be aware, that color vision deficiency will affect people's ability to see colors in your documents.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=258)** It's estimated that about eight percent of men and point five percent of women experience the most common form of this deficiency, which is red-green color vision deficiency.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=269)** So if you use color as an emphasis technique in a document, make sure that the emphasis is not critical to understanding the content.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=278)** If it is, you should choose a different emphasis technique.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=282)** It's best to pick one of these techniques and stick with it throughout the document.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=288)** Here's an example of a couple of paragraphs where the emphasis technique switches throughout.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=294)** This makes the text look pretty jumbled, it's visually confusing and overall, jarring.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=301)** Now, here's the same text where we chose to use only bold as our emphasis technique.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=307)** The effect is much more professional and overall, the page looks a lot less jumbled.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=314)** Also be careful that you're not over-signaling.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=316)** You only really need to use one of these techniques at a time.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=320)** Here's an example of emphasized text that uses three of the emphasis techniques we just talked about.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=326)** It's too much and it's unnecessary.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=330)** We emphasized, then emphasized again and then we emphasized again.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=336)** Using one emphasis technique at a time is more professional and helps the text to stand out without over-doing it.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=346)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=347)** There will be times when you want to emphasize some text in a document to make it stand out and draw a reader's attention.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=354)** There are many, many ways to do this effectively using a computer and desktop publishing software.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=362)** You want to avoid using underlines and all caps to indicate emphasis.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=366)** This will help your documents look more professional, more modern, and more visually pleasing.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=376)** That's law 17.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/17-avoid-all-caps-and-underlined-text?u=76281980&t=378)** Avoid all caps and underlined text.

#### 18 Set acronyms and initialisms in small caps
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 18, Set Acronyms and Initialisms in Small Caps.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=7)** Acronyms are abbreviations formed from the initial letters of other words, and acronyms are pronounced as words.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=17)** Initialisms are also abbreviations that are formed from the initial letters of other words, but initialisms are not pronounced as words.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=25)** They're pronounced as separate letters.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=28)** Examples of acronyms are NASA, which stands for National Aeronautics and Space Administration, ASCII, which stands for American Standard Code for Information Interchange, NATO, which stands for North Atlantic Treaty Organization, and ASAP which stands for as soon as possible.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=47)** And you can see we took the first letter of each of these words and strung it together to form an abbreviation which we pronounce as a word.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=59)** Examples of initialisms are CIA, which stands for Central Intelligence Agency, and here, instead of reading CIA as a word, we pronounce each separate letter, and that's what makes this an initialism.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=75)** FBI, which stands for Federal Bureau of Investigation, AM, which stands for ante meridiem, and PM, which stands for post meridiem.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=87)** In body text, you'll often see acronyms and initialisms set in all caps or all upper case letters, and usually they look like this, and notice how these abbreviations that are set in all caps overpower the other body text on the page.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=102)** These abbreviations look so huge and a little bit out of place.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=107)** So, you should avoid using all caps in professional documents for this reason.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=112)** Text that is set in all caps is really too heavy on the page, and it looks out of scale compared to the rest of the body text that's going to be set in mostly lower case letters.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=122)** So then, what do you do with acronyms and initialisms?
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=125)** What is the proper way to treat these things typographically?
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=129)** You should set them using small caps, and what are small caps?
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=134)** Small caps are upper case characters that are drawn at a lower case scale.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=140)** Small cap characters are about the same height as a typeface's x-height, but they're capital letters.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=147)** Here's an example of the same body text as before, but this time our abbreviations, our acronyms and our initialisms, have been set in small caps, rather than in all caps, like they were before, and notice how in our second example these abbreviations look so much nicer, and they really integrate better with the rest of the body text on the page and in the paragraph.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=171)** Acronyms and initialisms also look best with some letter spacing or a little extra space added between each letter.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=180)** Here's an example of the same text from earlier, that contains our abbreviations, and now they're set in small caps, and we've also added just a little bit of letter spacing, or a little bit of horizontal space between each letter.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=192)** Although acronyms and initialisms are abbreviations, they do not need periods included after each letter.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=201)** Including these periods adds unnecessary visual clutter to the abbreviations, and your pages are going to look a lot cleaner without all that extra punctuation.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=210)** The meaning is still clear.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=213)** Readability is not sacrificed without this extra punctuation.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=217)** If you're going to be using a lot of small caps in your document, you want to choose a type family that includes a small caps typeface.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=225)** Small caps typefaces have been specifically created and drawn to visually integrate with other text on your page and to look really good while doing it.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=235)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=237)** Pay attention to acronyms and initialisms in your documents.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=240)** You should set them using small caps.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=243)** Properly typesetting these abbreviations will go a long way toward making your pages look professional and aesthetically pleasing.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/18-set-acronyms-and-initialisms-in-small-caps?u=76281980&t=252)** That's Law 18, Set Acronyms and initialisms in Small Caps.
