#### 19 Hang punctuation in small chunks of text
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 19: Hang punctuation in small chunks of text.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=7)** When you have a small chunk of text that contains punctuation marks, the punctuation marks should be hung off of the aligned edge.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=15)** What does that mean?
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=17)** Hanging punctuation may be a new phrase that you've never heard before.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=22)** Let's look at a few examples to explain the concept.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=26)** In this first example, we've got a page from a document, and we're featuring a pull quote on this page.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=33)** The pull quote is helping us break up some of the text on the page and add some visual interest.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=39)** Notice how the opening quotation marks have been left-aligned with the rest of the text.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=43)** In this example, the punctuation is not hung.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=49)** In the second example, we've got the same text, but now the punctuation has been hung.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=55)** Do you see how the opening quotation mark is actually hung outside of the main text block?
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=61)** See how it's been scooted to the left just a bit?
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=65)** Looking at both of these examples, notice how the first line in this example appears to be indented just a bit.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=73)** This visual effect is being caused by the amount of white space that's below the opening quotation mark.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=80)** The text is mathematically aligned.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=82)** Everything is aligned left here.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=84)** But it doesn't look aligned.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=86)** So it's not correct.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=88)** It's not correctly aligned and needs to be fixed.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=92)** We can fix this issue by hanging the quotation mark, just like in the second example.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=98)** By moving the quotation mark just outside of the main text block like we did here, scooting it to the left, we've created a clean, hard edge with our text.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=108)** The left edge is strong and aligned.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=111)** The punctuation is hung, and the pull quote now looks correct.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=118)** Here's another example.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=119)** This time, we have some text on a poster.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=121)** This text has been right-aligned.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=123)** You'll notice it's a sentence and there's a period at the end of the sentence.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=127)** Now, the text is mathematically right-aligned.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=129)** There's an imaginary vertical line here and everything's lined up against it.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=134)** But notice how the period makes it look like the very last line is indented a bit.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=140)** Because a period is such a small character with so much white space on top, the period is taking a visual chunk out of our nice, clean right edge.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=149)** Here's the same text.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=150)** It's still right-aligned, still have the period.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=153)** But now the ending period has been hung off of the right edge.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=157)** Our strong right text edge is preserved.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=160)** And now the alignment looks correct.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=164)** This law applies to small chunks of text that have been separated out from body text in long documents.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=170)** This may be text like quotations, maybe headlines.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=175)** This law also applies to text on posters, business cards, invitations, and any other single page documents that don't contain large amounts of body text.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=186)** In body text, punctuation is usually not hung.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=191)** That's because body text is small enough that the visual interruption caused by punctuation is pretty minimal.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=198)** So you don't have to worry about hanging punctuation in body text.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=203)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=205)** When you're working with small chunks of text, punctuation should be hung in order to preserve proper visual alignment.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=212)** Hanging punctuation means moving punctuation marks outside of the text block so that the punctuation does not interfere with the text alignment.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=222)** This attention to detail will create strong, visually correct text alignments in your documents, and it's really going to take your documents to the next level professionally.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=235)** That's law 19.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/19-hang-punctuation-in-small-chunks-of-text?u=76281980&t=237)** Hang punctuation in small chunks of text.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (2)
> **Analogies:** just like (1)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 20 Hang bullets and numbers in lists
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 20: Hang bullets and numbers in lists.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=6)** Using bulleted or numbered lists is a great way to break up paragraphs of text and present information in an organized, chunked, way.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=15)** That's going to help readers digest it and better understand the information.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=20)** Numbered lists should be used when you're presenting a list of steps, or items in some kind of a sequence where the order of the items is important.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=31)** Bulleted lists should be used when you're presenting a list of information and order's not important at all.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=39)** Whether you're using bulleted lists or numbered lists, you should hang the bullets or numbers to preserve and create a strong aligned edge for the data in your lists.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=52)** What does it mean to hang bullets and numbers?
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=55)** Well, let's look at some examples to help explain the concept.

>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=62)** We've got some information here.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=63)** But the bullets have not been hung.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=66)** In the second example, we have the exact same list, but the difference here is that the bullets have been hung.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=72)** You can see that each bullet is actually set outside of the main text block.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=77)** The bullets have actually been scooted out to the left.

>

>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=124)** It looks professional and it looks correct.

> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=131)** Here's a numbered list, and the numbers have not been hung.

>

>

>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=172)** Now, this becomes really important when you have lists of information that contain single digits, double digits, and maybe even triple digit numbers.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=182)** Here's an example of a numbered list that contains 12 items.
>

>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=191)** But the numbers are not properly aligned.

> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=195)** Now, in this example, same list with the same list items, the numbers have also been hung off the left edge, but now the numbers are properly aligned.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=206)** They're aligned on the decimal point.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=208)** The numbers here create a strong right-edge alignment, and the text creates a strong left-edge alignment.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=216)** And now, the text and the numbers in this list are all correctly aligned and look great.

>

>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=249)** You want to learn how to properly use all the different tabs in your software, and that means using left-aligned tabs, right-aligned tabs, center-aligned tabs, and decimal-aligned tabs.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=260)** They all exist, and if you take the time and learn how to use them properly, you'll be so glad you did, and you're going to save yourself a lot of time and a lot of headaches.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=269)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=271)** Whenever you're setting bulleted and numbered lists, make sure that your text alignments are visually strong and intact.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=279)** Bullets should be aligned with bullets.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=281)** Numbers should be aligned with numbers.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=283)** And text should be aligned with text.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=287)** Numbers should always be aligned on their decimal points, even if these decimal points are not visible.
>

> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=294)** Hanging the bullets and numbers in your lists provides proper visual alignment for your text.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=301)** This attention to detail is going to take your documents to the next level professionally and they're going to look great.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/20-hang-bullets-and-numbers-in-lists?u=76281980&t=309)** That's law 20: Hang bullets and numbers in lists.

#### 21 Avoid bad line breaks
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 21: Avoid Bad Line Breaks.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=6)** A line break is the place in a paragraph where text breaks off at the end of one line and then continues again on the next line of the paragraph.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=16)** And there are Two Different Types of Bad Line Breaks.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=18)** Bad Visual Breaks and Bad Contextual Breaks.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=24)** Bad Visual Breaks are line breaks that Visually Disrupt the Flow of Your Text.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=31)** Here's an example of a small chunk of text that has some Bad Visual Breaks and here's an example of the same text, but now the breaks have been fixed and our paragraph looks a lot neater and a lot cleaner.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=44)** If you are using flush left text, like we are here in our examples, you want to make sure and pay attention to the right edge of your text block.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=53)** This first block of text here is an example of what is called a Bad Rag, or a Bad Ragged Edge.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=60)** And you can see over here on this right side the jagged dynamic shape that's being created in the white space of this right margin.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=70)** This is pretty Distracting and should be avoided.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=74)** Looking at the second sample block of text, notice how the Bad Rag has been fixed.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=79)** And we did this by making the entire text block just a little bit narrower.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=84)** This one small adjustment made a huge positive difference in the way that this text looks on the page.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=91)** Now the right edge is much less jagged and as a result the white space over here is a lot less distracting.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=99)** This isn't an issue that's just limited to flush left text.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=103)** You can also end up with Bad Breaks in Flush Right Text and also with Centered Text.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=111)** So watch out for Bad Visual Line Breaks.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=113)** They Make your Documents Look Less Professional, and they're Distracting to Readers.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=120)** Now let's talk about Bad Contextual Breaks.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=123)** Here's an example of a Bad Contextual Break in a headline and here's the same headline, but this time that break has been fixed.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=132)** In the first block of text, notice how the Line Break is splitting up words that really belong together in the same phrase and should appear on the same line.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=144)** If you're reading this headline, this Bad Break might cause you to go back and reread it, just to make sure you understood its meaning.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=152)** Any time that your layout forces readers to go back and reread something, this is a red flag that improvements need to be made.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=161)** Now when the break is fixed reading the text is so much easier and that's because we've minimized that distraction of the Bad Contextual Break.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=172)** You also want to watch your Line Breaks when you're dealing with URLs and Email Addresses.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=177)** It's really important that these special strings of text appear on the same line with no breaks, whenever possible.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=184)** And this can be a little tricky because URLs and Email Addresses tend to get long, some of them, but it's important because we want to make these special strings of text as readable and as useful as possible.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=197)** So how can you fix bad line breaks?
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=201)** Editing or rewriting the text is sometimes the best and the easiest way to fix these issues.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=208)** If you can add, delete, or maybe modify a few words sometimes you can take care of the bad break problem.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=216)** You may not always be able to edit the text in the document you're working on and if that's not an option, there are still a couple other ways that you can correct bad breaks.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=226)** You can Change the Width of the Text Block, make it a little bit wider, or a little bit narrower.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=234)** You can also Change the Size of the Text.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=238)** Often a very small, tiny change will make enough of a difference to fix the issues that you're having with bad breaks.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=247)** Let's summarize, Watch for Bad Line Breaks in Small Chunks of Text.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=253)** Fixing these Bad Line Breaks will Make Your Text Easier to Read, and Makes Your Documents Look More Professional.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/21-avoid-bad-line-breaks?u=76281980&t=262)** That's Law 21: Avoid Bad Line Breaks.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (7)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), break. (1), delete (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1), is called (1)
> **Warnings:** watch out (1)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)

#### 22 Use symbols and special characters as needed
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 22: Use symbols and special characters as needed.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=7)** There will be times when you need to include symbols and characters in your documents, that can't be found on your standard keyboard.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=15)** These characters include things like special punctuation marks, registered trademark symbols, copyright symbols, degree symbols, accented characters, and so on.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=27)** It can be really tempting to be a little lazy and not include these special characters in your documents, but they are not hard to use and should be included when needed.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=38)** This is especially true of what are called diacritical marks and diacritical marks are symbols added to letters of the alphabet that indicate a different pronunciation.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=51)** Accent marks are a type of diacritical mark that you're probably familiar with.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=56)** You can see on screen, some other examples of diacritical marks.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=61)** Now the English language does not contain many native words that use diacritical marks, but many, many other languages, including French, Italian, German, Spanish, and others, they do use a lot of diacritical marks.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=77)** The English language contains words that have been borrowed from some of these languages, that also require these diacritical marks.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=86)** If a word needs a diacritical mark to be spelled correctly, use it.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=93)** For example, in Spanish, the word si without an accent mark over the i, is not the same as the word si with an accent mark over the i.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=104)** This first word translates to if in English.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=108)** The second word with the accent mark translates to yes.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=113)** So you can see, it's really important to get these marks correct.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=117)** You could actually change the meaning of words by misusing or not including diacritical marks.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=125)** This is especially important when you're dealing with people's names.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=129)** Leaving off or misusing a necessary diacritical mark is like misspelling a person's name.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=135)** You do not want to do that in your documents.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=139)** Symbols include things like degree symbols, mathematical symbols, monetary symbols, and so on.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=147)** Depending on the type of documents that you create and the content that you're working with, you may frequently need to use some of these symbols, and so it's really important that you know how to do that and how to access these symbols.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=161)** There are a lot of different ways that you could access these symbols and accented characters on a computer.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=165)** If you use certain symbols and characters often enough, you'll memorize how to create them really quickly.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=172)** For the special characters that you haven't memorized, learn how to access special characters in your operating system, and in the software package that you're using.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=181)** Here are a few tips to help you get started.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=185)** On Macintosh computers, a really easy way to insert diacritical marks is to put your cursor in your document and hold down a letter on the keyboard.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=195)** Any diacritical marks that exist for that letter will appear and you simply select the one you want.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=201)** This works in email and it works in text messages on iPhones.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=206)** Further special characters on a Macintosh computer, you could use what's called the character viewer or the keyboard viewer.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=215)** If you use a computer with the Windows operating system, look for what's called the character map or the international keyboard utility.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=225)** For web designers, to make special characters and accented letters show up on your web pages, you're going to use a special set of codes that are called character entities.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=236)** You'll insert these character entities into your HTML code and then a web browser is going to display the corresponding symbols or characters that you coded.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=247)** You could do an online search for character entities and you will get plenty of information that gives you all of these special codes to use in your web pages.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=257)** Keep in mind that not all typefaces include these special characters.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=262)** The typeface designer has to create these characters and include them in the typeface.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=268)** Even though there are many, many websites out there where you can download free typefaces, many of these freebies do not include symbols and special characters.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=280)** If you're creating a document and you know you're going to need the use of special characters, before you pick a typeface, before you decide on the final typeface, make sure that the typefaces you're looking at contain the special characters that you need.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=297)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=300)** When you're typesetting and designing documents, be aware that the text may require the use of symbols and special characters that don't appear on a standard keyboard.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=310)** Don't be lazy and just ignore these special characters, they're not hard to include.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=316)** Learn how to access the special characters in the software and operating system that you're using.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=322)** If a word contains a special character, then it needs that character to be spelled correctly.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/22-use-symbols-and-special-characters-as-needed?u=76281980&t=330)** That's Law 22: Use symbols and special characters as needed.

#### 23 Use proportional old-style figures in body text
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 23: Use proportional oldstyle figures in body text.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=7)** You may not be aware of this, but some typefaces contain different types of figures that have been designed for different uses.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=15)** Figures is just another word for numbers.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=20)** Proportional figures have been designed to consume different amounts of space based on the shape of each number.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=27)** In proportional figures, the number one takes up less space than the number eight.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=34)** Proportional figures work well within lines of mixed-case text, like body text.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=40)** Another type of figures are tabular figures.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=44)** Tabular figures are numbers that have been created to take up the same amount of horizontal and vertical space.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=51)** A number one takes up exactly the same amount of space as a number eight, and every other number.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=59)** Because of this, tabular figures are great for creating tables of text because they stay aligned with each other.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=66)** Tabular figures are the numbers that you get automatically when you start typing numbers in a document.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=73)** Within each of these divisions, there are two more types of figures, oldstyle figures and lining figures.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=81)** Oldstyle figures have varying shapes and positions for each number.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=87)** Some of these numbers even have ascenders and some have descenders.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=92)** Lining figures all sit on the same baseline, and all lining figures have the same height, which is usually equal to the height of a capital letter.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=103)** This all may be a little confusing.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=105)** Let's look at some examples to try and clear this up.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=109)** Tabular lining figures should be used in tables of data because the numbers will automatically align both vertically and horizontally.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=118)** But tabular lining figures should not be used in strings of text, like body text.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=123)** They do not integrate well visually.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=127)** Tabular oldstyle figures are numbers that have fixed widths, but they have the varying heights of oldstyle figures.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=137)** Now, tabular oldstyle figures are best used when you're dealing with tabular data that could use a little sprucing up, or would benefit from being a little more fancy-looking.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=149)** Examples would include things like, if you're making table of contents, if you're creating lists of phone numbers on a business card or maybe letterhead.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=159)** But tabular oldstyle figures should not be used in strings of text, like body text.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=164)** They don't integrate well visually.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=169)** Proportional lining figures should be used for numbers that appear in strings of uppercase text, like headlines, because the proportional spacing helps them visually integrate with the text and their height is usually just about even with the uppercase letters.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=186)** Proportional lining figures should not be used in blocks of mixed-case text, like body text.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=191)** They're too tall.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=193)** And these proportional lining figures tend to overwhelm the lowercase letters that you see in body text.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=201)** The last type of figures that we're going to talk about are proportional oldstyle figures.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=205)** These are the figures that should be used in blocks of mixed-case text, like body text.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=212)** Because of their size and varying heights, they integrate really well with surrounding lowercase letters and they don't call too much attention to themselves, becoming a distraction.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=225)** All of these special figures can be found in what are called open type typefaces.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=230)** But you cannot access these figures using just your keyboard.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=234)** Accessing these special figures can be done in a couple different ways, depending on the software you're using.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=241)** If you're a web designer, you can use the font variant tag in your CSS to access these different types of figures.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=250)** You need to be aware that not all typefaces are going to include these special figures.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=255)** The type designer, whoever designed the typeface, had to have designed and included these figures when they created the typeface.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=264)** You're also going to need to choose an open type typeface in order to have access to these figures.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=270)** If you know that you are going to be creating a document that contains a lot of figures or a lot of numbers, it's a great idea to choose a typeface upfront that will include these varied numerals.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=284)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=287)** Using the proper figures in your text is a great way to take your documents to the next level professionally.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=293)** Learn which types of figures should be used when, and learn how to access these figures in the software that you're using.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=301)** Paying attention to details like this will help make your text more readable, and it also adds a certain level of sophistication and elegance to your documents.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=313)** That's law 23.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/23-use-proportional-old-style-figures-in-body-text?u=76281980&t=316)** Use proportional oldstyle figures in body text.

#### 24 Adjust leading and kerning for large text
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 24, Adjust Leading and Kerning for Large Text.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=6)** Let's start with a few definitions.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=8)** What is leading?
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=10)** Leading is the amount of vertical space between two lines of text, and leading is measured from the baseline of one line of text to the baseline of another.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=20)** Leading is also called line spacing.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=24)** Leading is automatically set by the software you're using, and the default amount of leading is usually 20 percent.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=31)** So, if you're using 10 point text, default leading is going to be 12 points.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=38)** What is kerning?
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=40)** Kerning is the amount of horizontal space between two letters.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=44)** Don't confuse kerning with tracking or letter spacing.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=48)** Tracking is uniform horizontal spacing across a range of characters, and remember kerning deals with the amount of horizontal spacing between just two specific letters.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=60)** Most typefaces have what are called built-in kerning tables, and these kerning tables, their job is to automatically adjust the amount of space between certain letter pairs.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=71)** Why do certain letter pairs need different amounts of kerning?
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=75)** Imagine for every character that you type that there's an invisible box that defines the borders of that character.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=83)** These invisible boxes don't usually overlap when you string characters together, because if they did overlap, then characters would start to appear on top of each other or would be very crowded together.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=95)** But for certain letter pairs, these invisible boxes need to overlap, and they need to overlap so that the characters look right on the page.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=104)** For example, let's look at this letter pair.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=107)** We've got a capital T and an E.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=110)** Because of the shape of each letter, the invisible boxes around each letter, if we obey those borders, they make these two letters appear to be too far apart.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=120)** A typeface's kerning table would contain a special rule for this letter pair, and that rule would automatically scoot the E back to the left, so that it would fit just under the crossbar of the T.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=133)** This would improve the look of this letter pair, and, therefore, it would improve the look of your document.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=139)** Examples of other letter pairs that often require extra kerning are AV, Vo, To, Ta, Wa,
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=151)** and W with a period.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=155)** If leading and kerning are automatically handled by software, why do we need to adjust them?
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=163)** Let's look at some examples.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=165)** For body text, default leading is usually just fine.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=170)** There may be times when you want to play with leading for certain effects.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=174)** For example, you might want to add more vertical space to give a block of text a little extra air.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=180)** You'll often see people add leading on documents like business cards or on invitations to jazz it up, to add a little bit of fanciness to the document.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=192)** Text set at large sizes may also benefit from leading adjustments.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=196)** Large text usually needs less leading, and large text with few descenders needs even less leading.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=205)** So, here's an example of text that's been set at two different sizes, one small and one large.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=211)** On the first example, the text looks just fine.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=214)** The leading looks good.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=215)** But when the text gets larger, the lines look like they're too far apart.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=220)** The leading needs to be tightened up.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=222)** Here's the same large text, and this time the leading's been tweaked.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=227)** See how much better that looks?
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=228)** We just tightened up that leading, and now our text looks better.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=232)** It looks more cohesive.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=235)** Small chunks of text that are set in all caps also require less leading in order to look right, and that's because of the lack of descenders in all cap characters.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=245)** Here's a headline that is set in all caps, and we've used default leading, and because there are no descenders in the all capital letters, the lines of the text look way too far apart.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=259)** Here's the same headline, but this time the leading's been tweaked.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=262)** We've tightened it up, and now our headline looks a lot better.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=267)** Similarly with kerning, in body text you usually don't need to worry about kerning.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=273)** As long as you're using a professionally designed typeface, the kerning table should do its job, and you will not have to get involved.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=281)** A special note, if you do use Microsoft Word, kerning is not applied by default.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=287)** It's actually not turned on.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=288)** You have to go in and turn on kerning.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=291)** It's found in the font menu of Microsoft Word.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=295)** When text is set at large sizes, kerning may need to be adjusted between certain letter pairs, in order for these characters to look correct and look right on the page.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=305)** Here's an example of some text that's been set at two different sizes.

> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=308)** Notice how everything looks just fine at the smaller size.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=311)** The kerning tables are doing their job.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=313)** All the letters in the text look correct and evenly spaced.

>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=318)** But in the larger version, some of the letters look farther apart than the other letters, like these here.
>

>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=327)** They just don't look right.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=329)** So, let's fix it.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=331)** Here, the letter pair has now been manually kerned, and it looks right.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=335)** It looks correct.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=336)** Now, all the letters look evenly spaced, and none of those letter pairs are negatively calling attention to themselves, like they were before.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=346)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=348)** It's really important to pay attention to text leading and kerning so that you're aware when it needs to be adjusted, and this is especially important when you're using text that's being set at larger type sizes.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=362)** Text that uses proper leading and kerning looks much more professional and is easier to read.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=367)** This attention to detail and proper typesetting allows readers to focus on the content's message, instead of being distracted by bad formatting.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/24-adjust-leading-and-kerning-for-large-text?u=76281980&t=378)** That's Law 24, Adjust Leading and Kerning for Large Text.

#### 25 Verify software alignments optically
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 25, verify software alignments optically.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=6)** When you're designing documents, you're often going to want to align objects on a page.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=10)** So for example, you might want to center text or images on a page.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=15)** And, there may be times when you want to align objects to each other.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=19)** The problem is that while your software can mathematically align these objects, right now the software is not smart enough to know if these alignments look correct.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=30)** Sometimes the alignments that your software is doing, the mathematical alignments, are just fine.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=36)** And other times they're not, and they need manual adjustments.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=41)** They need a little bit of human intervention.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=44)** It all depends on the objects you're aligning and what they look like.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=49)** Here's a logo that was designed for a non-profit group in Houston, TX.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=53)** And, whenever I want to horizontally center this logo on a page, I have to be really careful because, you can see in this example, the logo is mathematically, horizontally centered on the page, but it does not look centered.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=68)** It looks like it's just a little too far to the left, and it actually looks like it might tip over just a little bit.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=76)** Why? Because the sun rays are visually lighter than the rest of the logo, which means that the left side of this logo is visually heavier than the right side.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=88)** When I'm centering this logo on a page, it needs to be centered using just the text, not including the sun rays.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=97)** So, let's take a look and see what that does.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=99)** In this example, I have compensated for this alignment issue, and I've moved the logo a bit to the right.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=108)** You'll notice now it looks correctly horizontally centered.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=113)** And there's no way that your software program can do this, at least not yet.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=118)** This is the kind of thing that you really have to see with your eyes, and then go in and manually fix it.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=126)** Another issue to watch out for when you're dealing with alignments are ascenders and descenders, especially when you're vertically centering text on a page.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=136)** If you're using a typeface with extreme proportions and large ascenders and descenders, this becomes even more important.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=144)** So here's some sample text on a business card.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=146)** And this text is mathematically centered on the card both horizontally and vertically by the software.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=153)** But, it doesn't look quite right, it doesn't look centered. Why?
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=157)** Because, the word's that we're using, have descenders but no ascenders.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=164)** So, horizontally this looks just fine, but vertically it looks too high on the page.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=170)** The text looks like it's too high up.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=174)** The descenders of this typeface are pretty large and they're visually light.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=178)** They're thin, they're a little wispy, but our software doesn't know that.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=181)** So, we need to manually move this text down on the card, to make it look visually centered.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=190)** There may also be times when you need to tweak horizontal alignments in your text based on specific characters.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=198)** Here's an example of some text that's left aligned, and the first line starts with what's called a dropped cap.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=205)** And a dropped cap is just a dropped capital letter.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=208)** It's used to add visual interest, maybe spice up the page a bit.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=213)** And, in this example, our drop cap, or the first letter, is the letter capital T.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=220)** This text block is mathematically left aligned including our drop cap, but visually it just doesn't look quite right.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=227)** We've lost our nice hard left edge.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=232)** Now the second example, we've tweaked the alignment, and now the crossbar of the T, hangs just outside of the text block.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=239)** You can see it's been scooted to the left just a little bit.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=242)** And, you can see how that slight adjustment gives a lot more strength to this left edge of text.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=252)** Our goal is left horizontal alignment.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=255)** If the text does not look properly aligned, if it doesn't look like it's left aligned, then it's not correct and you need to fix it.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=263)** Don't just rely on the software to make all of these choices for you.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=270)** Let's summarize, do not blindly trust software alignments.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=276)** You might have done everything right in the software, and the software may be doing its job and mathematically aligning everything on your page.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=283)** But you still need to use your eyes and judge alignments for yourself.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=288)** Then, you can make manual adjustments as needed.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=292)** Always check objects that are supposed to be aligned and make sure that they're optically aligned.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=297)** Alignments need to look correct in order to be correct.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/25-verify-software-alignments-optically?u=76281980&t=302)** That's Law 25, verify software alignments optically.
