### 5. 4. How to Use Punctuation Properly

#### 26 Connect thoughts, using em dashes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 26: Connect Thoughts Using Em Dashes What is an em dash?
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=8)** An em dash is the widest dash character.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=12)** On a typewriter, and even still on a standard keyboard on your computer, there is no em dash character.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=18)** So, typists would use double hyphens in order to stand in for an em dash, knowing that the typesetter would go back and replace their double hyphens with an actual em dash.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=30)** But assuming that you're using a computer to create documents, you should not be using double hyphens ever.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=36)** There is no need.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=38)** Double hyphens are not correct, and they make your documents look unprofessional.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=44)** If you find yourself wanting to use double hyphens, try inserting an em dash instead.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=50)** And if you see any double hyphens in documents that were created by someone else, replace them with em dashes.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=57)** So when should you use an em dash?
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=60)** Em dashes can be handy when you don't want a comma, which would end the thought, but you aren't quite sure what punctuation to use instead.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=68)** Em dashes can replace commas, semicolons, colons and parentheses.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=75)** Em dashes are often used to indicate added emphasis.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=80)** Em dashes can also be used to indicate long pauses, or interruptions in text.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=86)** Em dashes are also used to show abrupt changes of thought.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=93)** You should not add spaces around an em dash, but depending on the typeface that you're using, the size of the typeface, and the characters surrounding the dash, you may need to add what are called thin spaces on either side of an em dash, between the dash and the characters on either side.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=111)** And a good rule of thumb is that if the dash touches, or almost touches, any characters, then a thin space is needed.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=120)** And a thin space is just what it sounds like.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=122)** It's a space that's really thin.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=124)** It's actually about a fifth of an em wide.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=127)** And depending on the software you're using, you may be able to insert a thin space by using a menu, but if that's not the case you can create your own thin spaces by typing a regular space, and then selecting it and making that space smaller.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=144)** So how can you create em dashes?
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=146)** Well, em dashes are not characters that are included on a standard keyboard.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=150)** So they're considered special characters, and you're going to need to use a special menu, or a special key combination, in order to insert an em dash into your documents.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=161)** If you're using a Macintosh computer, you can insert an em dash into your document by holding down the keys option and shift, and then typing a hyphen.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=173)** And if you're using a Windows machine, you can insert an em dash by holding down the alt key, and then typing the numbers "0151".
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=184)** Now if you're a web designer in HTML, you would use this character entity in order to insert an em dash.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=192)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=194)** Stop using double hyphens.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=196)** They're not correct and they make your documents look unprofessional.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=201)** Instead, use an em dash when you want to add emphasis to a word or phrase, to indicate a long pause or interruption, or to show abrupt changes of thought.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=215)** Em dashes are really easy to insert in your documents, and they will make your documents more professional.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/26-connect-thoughts-using-em-dashes?u=76281980&t=223)** That's Law 26: Connect Thoughts Using Em Dashes.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (3), find (1)
> **Code Keywords:** else, (1), let (1)
> **Env Vars:** html (1)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1)
> **Best Practices:** rule of thumb (1)

#### 27 Show ranges, using en dashes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 27.
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=1)** Show Ranges Using En Dashes.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=6)** What is an en dash?
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=8)** An en dash is a dash that's larger than an hyphen but smaller than an em dash.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=15)** Now, many people aren't familiar with en dashes and when to use them.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=19)** When people use typewriters, the choices are really between using a hyphen or a double hyphen.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=26)** There's nothing in between, on a typewriter.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=28)** But the en dash is that in-between character and it does have important uses.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=34)** What are those uses?
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=36)** And when should you use an en dash?
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=39)** Well, most commonly, en dashes are used to show ranges of time, ranges of duration or ranges of distance.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=47)** So, for example, if you're talking about January through June, 2 o'clock to 5 o'clock, Monday through Friday, 2001 to 2003, pages 230 to 255 or the Florida-Texas Flight Path.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=66)** In all of those cases, we're talking about ranges and an en dash is the proper character to use.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=75)** En dashes are also used to join words when at least one word is an open compound.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=81)** Open compounds are usually two-word proper nouns.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=85)** So, for example, if you're talking about the New York-New Jersey Border or Puerto Rican-United States Agreement or the North Carolina-Virginia Border.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=98)** You can see, in all of those examples, we're joining words and phrases and at least one word is an open compound.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=107)** En dashes are also used to join hyphenated compounds.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=111)** So, if you have two hyphenated words that you're joining together, we would use an en dash to do the joining.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=118)** For example, post-Jacksonian-pre-Nixonian politics, you've got two hyphenated phrases and an en dash in between to join them.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=130)** You should not add spaces around an en dash but, depending on the typeface that you're using, the size of the typeface and the characters that are surrounding the dash, you may need to add what are called thin spaces on either side of an en dash.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=147)** A good rule of thumb is that if the dash touches, or almost touches, any character then a thin space is needed.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=157)** A thin space is really just what it sounds like it's a space that's thinner than normal and a thin space is actually about a fifth of an em wide.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=166)** Now, depending on the software they are using, you may be able to insert a thin space just by using a menu or maybe a menu choice, but, if that's not the case, you can create your own thin spaces by typing a space highlighting it and making it smaller.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=182)** How do you create en dashes?
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=185)** Well, en dashes are not characters that are included on a standard keyboard.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=189)** So, they're considered special characters.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=192)** You're going to need to use a special menu or a special key combination in order to create an en dash.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=199)** On a Mac, you can create an en dash by holding down the option key and typing a hyphen.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=206)** If you're using a computer with the Windows operating system, you can create an en dash by holding down the alt key and typing the numbers 0 1 5 0.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=218)** In HTML, if you're a web designer, the character entity for an en dash is this: Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=227)** Stop using hyphens when you really need an en dash instead.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=232)** Use an en dash when you want to show ranges, join open compound words, or join hyphenated compounds.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=242)** En dashes are easy to insert into your documents and they will make your documents more professional.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=250)** That's Law 27.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/27-show-ranges-using-en-dashes?u=76281980&t=253)** Show Ranges Using En Dashes.

#### 28 Clarify, and improve readability, using hyphens
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 28: Clarify and improve readability using hyphens.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=6)** Hyphens are dash characters that are available on all standard keyboards.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=10)** So hyphens are not special characters.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=13)** Because of this, hyphens are the dash character that you're probably the most familiar with.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=20)** Hyphens are less wide than both em dashes and en dashes.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=25)** Although most people are familiar with hyphens, their usage can be a little bit confusing and sometimes complicated.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=35)** Writers and editors are really the people who are responsible for including hyphens in the text that you may be typesetting.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=43)** So, as a desktop publisher, what do you need to know about hyphens and when to use them?
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=48)** Hyphens should be used to improve readability and clarify meaning.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=53)** Let's look at a few examples.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=55)** Hyphens are used when two or more words are acting as a single modifier and appear before a noun.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=62)** An example of this is five-dollar bills.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=64)** Using the hyphen here makes it clear that we're talking about multiple bills that are worth five dollars each.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=71)** Because we join the words "five" and "dollar" with a hyphen, it's clear that both of those words are acting as a single phrase to modify or describe the word "bills."
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=84)** The phrase five dollar bills, with no hyphens, is describing very different.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=89)** This phrase is describing five one-dollar bills.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=93)** Without the hyphen, the word "five" is assumed to be modifying or describing the words "dollar bills."
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=102)** Let's look at some other examples where hyphens can help clarify meaning or make words easier to read.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=110)** You should use a hyphen when you're joining a letter with a word, like A-frame or X-ray.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=116)** Without the hyphen, these words could cause some difficulty when reading in a document.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=123)** You should use a hyphen when you're using the prefix re and you mean "to do over."
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=130)** An employee who re-signs is very different from an employee who resigns.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=137)** Use a hyphen if you're joining a prefix with a proper noun.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=141)** An example is anti-American.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=144)** The uppercase letter of the proper noun, here, American, without a hyphen, makes this a strange-looking word.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=152)** Use a hyphen if you're joining a prefix with a root word and the prefix ends in the same letter that the root word starts with.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=161)** An example is ultra-ambitious, or non-native.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=165)** You can see that the a in "ultra" and the a in "ambitious," the n in "non" and the n in "native" both repeat.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=172)** Without the hyphens, these words are pretty clumsy and difficult to read.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=178)** Use a hyphen if you're joining two words that contain the same three letters in a row, such as cross-section.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=186)** Again, without the hyphen, this word becomes a little clumsy and definitely more difficult to read.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=193)** You should hyphenate all numbers between 21 and 99.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=198)** This helps make it clear that two words are being combined to form one number.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=204)** Use hyphens in phone numbers and social security numbers.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=208)** Here, hyphens are being used to chunk these strings of numbers and to help clearly define them for readers.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=214)** If you're in the United States and you see this string of numbers, you would know right away that this is probably a telephone number.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=221)** But without the hyphens, this string becomes a lot less obvious.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=227)** Also use a hyphen if a person has two last names that are being joined together.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=233)** It's important to note that a hyphen is not the same as a minus sign.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=238)** If you're creating a document that contains mathematical formulas, don't use a hyphen if what you really need is a minus sign.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=245)** A minus sign is a special character that's included in most professionally-designed typefaces.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=251)** Notice how the hyphen looks really small.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=255)** The minus sign's been properly sized for the numbers that surround it.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=261)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=263)** Hyphens can be confusing little bits of punctuation.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=266)** To help clarify the confusion, remember to use a hyphen if it improves readability or clarifies meaning.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=274)** When in doubt, you can always consult a dictionary or a style guide.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=279)** You may find conflicting hyphenation rules between these sources.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=283)** People don't always agree on what should be hyphenated and what shouldn't.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=287)** But what's most important is consistency.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=290)** Pick a rule and stick with it throughout your documents.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=295)** That's law 28.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/28-clarify-and-improve-readability-using-hyphens?u=76281980&t=298)** Clarify and improve readability using hyphens.

#### 29 Designate feet and inches, with prime symbols
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=0)** Law 29, Designate Feet and Inches with Prime Symbols.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=7)** So what are prime symbols?
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=9)** This is what a single prime symbol looks like and this is what a double prime symbol looks like.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=15)** These character are different than curly quotes, which look like this.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=20)** And they're also different from straight quotes which look like this.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=24)** So, let's briefly discuss each of these characters and their uses.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=30)** Straight quotes are a hold-over from the days of the typewriter, and should not be used for anything.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=36)** In fact, modern word processors and page layout programs will automatically use curly quotes as you type.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=44)** There's usually a setting somewhere in the software preferences, and the setting is almost always automatically turned on and that tells the software to use curly quotes instead of straight.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=56)** And this is a really good thing because curly quotes are prettier than straight quotes, and curly quotes have been designed by the typeface's designer to flow with the letters on the page and in your documents.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=70)** Curly quotes have always been used in professional typography and should always be used in your documents as well.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=77)** So, when is that?
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=79)** When should you use curly quotes, and maybe more importantly, when should you not use them?
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=85)** Curly quotes should always be used when quoting material.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=89)** And in America, we use double quotes to open and close quotations.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=95)** We use single opening and closing quotes when we're quoting material that appears inside other quoted material, and these are called nested quotes because one quote is nested inside another.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=109)** Single closing quotes are also used as apostrophes.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=114)** So, make sure your software is correctly using curly quotes for these symbols.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=119)** Remember, you don't ever need to use straight quotes.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=123)** But, curly quotes are not always correct, either.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=127)** If you are working with information that contains measurements with feet and inches, then you need to be using prime symbols, not curly quotes.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=135)** This is not correct.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=139)** Single prime symbols are used to show feet measurements, and double prime symbols are used to show inch measurements.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=147)** The prime symbol has other uses, in math, music, linguistics, and a bunch of other fields.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=154)** So, if you are a designer who works with documents that cover these topics, then you should definitely become familiar with prime symbols and how to use them.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=164)** Earlier, we talked about the fact that modern word processing and desktop publishing software automatically creates curly quotes when you type a quote symbol on your keyboard.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=174)** If you're using a piece of software that's not automatically inserting curly quotes, you can always access these characters through special key combinations.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=183)** So, if you're on a Macintosh computer, you would hold down the option key and type a closing square bracket for an opening single curly quote.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=192)** Hold down the option and shift keys and type a closing square bracket for a closing single curly quote.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=199)** Hold down the option key and type an opening square bracket for an opening double curly quote, and hold down the option and shift keys and type an opening square bracket for a closing double curly quote.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=214)** If you're on a computer that uses the Windows operating system, you would hold down the alt key and type the numbers zero, one, four, five for an opening single curly quote, hold down the alt key and type zero, one, four, six for a closing single curly quote, hold down the alt key and type zero, one, four, seven for an opening double curly quote, and hold down the alt key and type zero, one, four, eight for a closing double curly quote.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=245)** What do you do if you don't want curly quotes but instead, you need to use prime symbols?
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=250)** Well, prime symbols are special characters, and that means that they're not directly accessible from your keyboard on your computer.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=259)** There are different ways, a lot of different ways, that you can display prime symbols, and you can always do a web search to learn more and find out more about the different ways, but here are some simple ways that'll at least help you get started.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=273)** If you are using a Macintosh computer, you can create a single prime symbol by holding down the option and shift keys and typing the letter E.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=281)** You can create a double prime symbol by holding down the option and shift keys and typing the letter G.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=288)** If you're on a computer that's using the Windows operating system, you create a single prime symbol by holding down the alt key and typing the numbers eight, two, four, two.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=300)** You can create a double prime symbol in a Windows operating system by holding down the alt key and typing the numbers eight, two, four, three.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=309)** Now, if you are a web designer, curly quotes may not be automatically generated like they are in word processing programs, and so, for the web, here are some different character entities for the characters we've been talking about.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=322)** For your curly quotes, for opening double quotation marks that are curly, use this character entity.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=329)** For closing double quotation marks that are curly, use this character entity.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=335)** For curly opening single quotes, use this character entity, and for closing single quotes, and also for apostrophes, use this character entity.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=347)** So, for a single prime symbol, if you're working on a website, you would use this character entity, and for a double prime symbol, use this character entity.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=359)** So, let's summarize.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=360)** Do not use straight quotes in your documents.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=363)** Instead, you are going to need to use curly quotes or prime symbols.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=368)** And it's really important to know when it's correct to use each of these different characters.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=375)** Curly quotes are used for quotations and apostrophes.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=379)** Prime symbols are used for measurements, like feet and inches, and prime symbols also have other applications in fields like mathematics, music, linguistics, and other fields as well.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/29-designate-feet-and-inches-with-prime-symbols?u=76281980&t=394)** That's law 29, Designate Feet and Inches with Prime Symbols.

> **Code Keywords:** this. (2), let (2), type. (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1), find (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), means that (1)
> **Cross-References:** we talked about (1)

#### 30 Replace missing characters with apostrophes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 30, Replace Missing Characters With Apostrophes.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=6)** Keep in mind that an apostrophe is always a single, closing, curly quotation mark.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=12)** If you're using modern word processing or desktop publishing software, you probably automatically get a single curly quote when you type an apostrophe on your keyboard.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=23)** There is a setting in the preferences of your software, and that makes this automatic, it makes the curly quote automatic, but it's a good idea to check and make sure, because you don't ever want to be using straight quotes, ever.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=38)** Curly quotes, or curly apostrophes, have been designed by the typeface's designer to flow with the text on the page, and also to look visually consistent with all the other content and all the other characters in a specific typeface.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=56)** Straight quotes are very harsh, they're disruptive.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=61)** Straight quotes are basically a holdover from typewriter days, and should not be used for anything, ever.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=67)** No double straight quotes and no single straight quotes.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=71)** As a typographer, your main concern with apostrophes is making sure that the correct character is being used in the correct place, so let's look at a few examples to hopefully explain this a little better.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=85)** Most people know that apostrophes are used in contractions, so "did not" becomes "didn't" and "is not" becomes "isn't."
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=93)** In these examples we've taken out a letter, and we've replaced it with an apostrophe.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=98)** Here, in both examples we've taken out the letter "o" and we've simply replaced the "o" with an apostrophe.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=104)** Similarly, if you're trying to make a sign that says "Gone Fishin'" an apostrophe would be used to show that you've removed the "g" from the end of the word "Fishing."
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=116)** You've replaced the letter "g" with an apostrophe.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=121)** If you're using a phrase like "Bread 'n' Butter" in a document, the correct way to typeset this phrase would be this.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=128)** You can see that we've got the letter "n" and we've got an apostrophe to the left of the "n" and an apostrophe to the right of the "n."
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=136)** You've removed two letters here, you removed the "a" and the "d" from the word "and," and so we've used an apostrophe in place of each of those missing letters.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=146)** When you type this phrase on a keyboard in your word processing or page layout software, your software's going to do this.
>

>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=166)** We're not trying to show here that the letter "n" is being quoted, and that's what we've done by enclosing the letter "n" in quotes.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=173)** Remember that an apostrophe is always a single closing quotation mark, never an opening quotation mark.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=181)** In this case, we want to show that we've removed two letters, so we want two apostrophes.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=187)** The problem is that your software package is not smart enough to know that this is your intent.

>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=206)** This is the correct way to typeset this phrase.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=212)** Here's another common issue with apostrophes.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=215)** If your document contains this sentence, and it is typeset this way, this is not correct.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=222)** The correct way to write this sentence is this.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=226)** You can see in our first example that we've got an apostrophe before the letter "s" and that is incorrect placement.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=235)** That placement shows possession, it shows that that decade, "the 40's," is owning something, and in this case that's not true.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=243)** So we don't need an apostrophe before the "s."
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=245)** We need an apostrophe in front of the decade, in front of the number "4," because here we've removed two characters, we've removed the number "1" and the number "9," and we need to replace those two characters with an apostrophe.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=259)** Now, we did remove two characters, but in this case, because they're consecutive, or they're in a row, we don't need two apostrophes, we can just use one apostrophe, and it's fine.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=269)** Again, make sure you're using an apostrophe here and not a single opening quote.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=274)** This is not correct, and this may be what your software does automatically.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=280)** Remember that apostrophes are always closing quotation marks, not opening quotation marks.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=287)** Apostrophes are not special characters, and are accessible from a standard keyboard.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=292)** But keep in mind that curly quotes, or curly apostrophes, may not always show up in the software that you're using.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=300)** So if you're using a piece of software, and it's not automatically inserting curly apostrophes, you can use special key combinations to access this character.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=309)** On a Macintosh, you would create a curly apostrophe by holding down the Option and Shift keys and then typing a right square bracket, or a closing square bracket.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=320)** And on a computer with the Windows operating system, you would hold down the Alt key and type the numbers 0146.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=330)** If you are a Web designer, curly quotes may not be automatically generated like they are in a word processing program, and so if you're designing Web pages, you would want to use this character entity for a curly, single, closing quote, or an apostrophe.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=351)** I want to just take a moment and talk about a personal pet peeve of mine.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=355)** I see this issue so often, that I really feel like it's worth discussing here, to try and clear up some confusion once and for all.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=363)** The issue is "it's" with an apostrophe versus "its" without an apostrophe.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=370)** If you don't remember anything else about apostrophes, please remember this.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=376)** "It's" with an apostrophe is short for "it is" or "it has."
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=380)** Remember, an apostrophe shows that characters have been taken out.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=385)** Read your sentence out loud, and if you could say "it is" or "it has" then use "it's" with an apostrophe.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=392)** In all other cases, all other cases, use "its" without an apostrophe.
>
> **[6:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=399)** I'm really hopeful that that will genuinely clear this up, and keep you from making this mistake.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=404)** I know that this is the job of the writer or the copy editor, to put these apostrophes in the right place, but I see this so often that it's pretty clear to me that lots and lots of people do not understand this rule.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=418)** So I really do want to help end the confusion.
>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=422)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=424)** Apostrophes are used to replace missing characters.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=428)** And apostrophes are always single closing quotes.
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=432)** Apostrophes are not special characters, and that means they're accessible from your standard keyboard, but you want to make sure that your software is correctly set to use curly quotes instead of straight quotes.
>
> **[7:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/30-replace-missing-characters-with-apostrophes?u=76281980&t=445)** That's Law 30, Replace Missing Characters With Apostrophes.

### 6. 5. How to Choose Typefaces

#### 31 Limit typefaces to two per document
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 31, limit typefaces to two per document.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=6)** You may have hundreds of typefaces on your computer and there are hundreds of thousands more typefaces out in the world that are available for purchase and some of them are even free to download.

> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=19)** With all of these typefaces at your fingertips, it's really easy to over do it and junk up your documents with too many typefaces in one document.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=31)** Using too many typefaces results in a document that's unprofessional and messy.

> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=111)** It looks like a professionally produced document.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=115)** In this example here, we chose to use one Serif typeface and one Sans Serif typeface.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=121)** And this is a great place to start.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=124)** Serif typefaces have little feet that appear at the end of each stroke.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=129)** And these little feet are called serifs.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=132)** In French, the word "sans" means without.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=135)** And so Sans Serif means without serifs.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=140)** And therefore a Sans Serif typeface does not have the little serifs at the end of each stroke.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=147)** You can see when you're looking at this document that we've created, that the Serif and the Sans Serif typeface are visually pretty different from each other.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=156)** And when you're choosing typefaces to use in your documents, you want to chose typefaces that are noticeably different from each other.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=164)** Why is that?
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=166)** Sameness competes but differences contrast and highlight.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=171)** And those are our goals when you're choosing different typefaces for a document.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=177)** Here's that same flyer again.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=178)** But this time, we've redesigned it and we're using two Sans Serif typefaces.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=184)** These are two different typefaces.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=185)** They're different, but they're not different enough.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=189)** And because they look so much alike, it's hard to tell if this was a mistake.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=194)** The sameness of these typefaces actually makes them compete with each other.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=198)** And it makes people focus on the typefaces themselves rather than the content.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=205)** We'd be better off, if we had chosen just a single Sans Serif typeface for the entire document.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=211)** It's much cleaner.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=213)** That's a less visually distracting solution.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=216)** And it's also a perfectly legitimate solution.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=219)** You don't have to use two typefaces in your documents.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=223)** But you don't want to use more than two unless you have some good design education or some good experience.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=232)** Let's go back to our example where we used two Sans Serif typefaces.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=236)** And we're going to compare this with our flyer that we looked at before where we chose one Serif and one Sans Serif typeface.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=244)** The contrast between these two typefaces is very clear, very obvious.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=249)** The typefaces are different, in fact they're very different from each other.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=252)** This creates a very nice amount of contrast.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=256)** The typefaces are different enough, that they aren't competing with each other.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=261)** Instead they're actually complimenting each other and highlighting the differences between the different types of text in our flyer.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=271)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=272)** Limiting yourself to two typefaces per document is an easy, instant way to make your documents look more professional.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=281)** You want to chose your two typefaces wisely though.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=284)** And make sure that there's enough difference or contrast between the typefaces in order to add visual interest and help with organization.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=294)** That's law 31.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/31-limit-typefaces-to-two-per-document?u=76281980&t=297)** Limit typefaces to two per document.

#### 33 Choose serif or sans serif, based on aesthetics
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=0)** - Law 33: Choose Serif or Sans Serif Based on Aesthetics.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=6)** Let's define serif and sans serif typefaces and look at the differences between them.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=12)** Serif typefaces have little lines, or what are called serifs that appear at the end of each stroke.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=19)** In French, the word sans means without, and so sans serif means without serifs and therefore, a sans serif typeface does not have the little serifs at the end of each stroke.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=33)** Many designers love to discuss the legibility of serif versus sans serif typefaces and you can find a slew of articles stating that serif typefaces are easier to read and then you can find an equal number of articles stating that sans serif typefaces are easier to read.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=54)** You can find articles that recommend sans serif typefaces for screen reading and serif for print reading and on and on and on.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=62)** In this debate, the Serif vs Sans Serif debate, can make for some great party conversation if you're hanging out with a bunch of people who love design.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=73)** But, scientific research has shown that at sizes larger than 10 points, there is no performance difference between serif and sans serif typefaces.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=83)** So, that means if you're using a typeface larger than 10 points, there is no scientific evidence, none, that serif typefaces are any more or any less legible than sans serif typefaces and vice versa.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=99)** Now, for type sizes that are smaller than 10 points, sans serif typefaces have an advantage.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=104)** They are more legible, especially on computer monitors.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=108)** Sans serif typefaces have uniform stroke width and simpler character shapes.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=116)** And, these attributes help sans serif typefaces remain legible at smaller sizes.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=122)** If you are setting Reversed Text, which is light text on a dark background, medium-weight, slightly larger sans serif typefaces are easier to read than some serif typefaces.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=137)** Serif typefaces can also work when reversed, but you wanna make sure and pick a serif typeface where the serifs are thick and uniform and shape.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=147)** Thin, delicate wispy serifs tend to disappear when reversed out.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=152)** That dark background just swallows them up.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=155)** So, stop worrying about whether to use a serif or sans serif typeface based on legibility.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=161)** It's much more important, much more important, to choose typefaces based on aesthetic preference and appropriateness for your document.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=170)** You wanna focus on the mood and personality of your document and then choose a typeface that is legible and reinforces these attributes.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=180)** Sans serif typefaces are usually perceived as having a more modern contemporary feel, while serif typefaces are usually perceived as more conservative and a little more traditional.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=194)** Let's summarize.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=196)** There is no scientific evidence that serif typefaces are any more or less legible than sans serif typefaces at sizes larger than 10 points.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=206)** Choose specific typefaces that are legible, aesthetically pleasing and that reinforce the mood and personality of your document.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=217)** In order to maximize the legibility of text in your document, focus on factors that do influence text legibility and these are things like the size of the text, the contrast between text and background, x-height, letter spacing and stroke width.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/33-choose-serif-or-sans-serif-based-on-aesthetics?u=76281980&t=235)** That's Law 33: Choose Serif or Sans Serif Based on Aesthetics.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** find (3), make (2)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2)
> **Speakers:** - law (1)


### 7. Conclusion

#### Goodbye
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=0)** - I hope you enjoyed learning The 33 Laws of Typography.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=4)** Abiding by these laws will make sure that typography works for you and not against you.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=11)** These laws will also help you communicate using text to maximum effect.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=18)** Keep in mind that you may not be able to follow all 33 of these laws at all times, in every document.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=24)** There may be times when you have to break one law in order to follow another.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=31)** Here's an example of an image caption in a document.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=34)** And you can see, this image caption contains less than seven characters on the last line.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=40)** When you're dealing with narrow blocks of text like this, it's not uncommon to end up with these dangling lines of text.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=47)** The width of this caption is the same, it's consistent with all other image captions in our document.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=54)** And that's important, because we want to maintain consistency in our document, remember, that's Law 4.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=60)** But we also want to have more than seven characters on all lines of text, that's Law 11.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=68)** In this case, assuming that we cannot rewrite this caption, I would choose to live with the dangling last line of text.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=76)** We're so close to seven characters, and the width of the text block is so narrow, that it's not too unsightly, and it's not causing too much visual disruption.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=87)** Another option would be to change the width of all the image captions in your document, and that could work as well, you could make the image captions a little narrower or a little wider in order to fix this issue.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=99)** But keep in mind, you may fix the dangling line in this instance, of this caption, but you may wind up creating dangling lines in other captions, other places in your document.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=113)** If you follow these 33 laws, and only break them when you have to, then you're going to be in good shape.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=121)** And when you have to choose between two laws, make the decision that results in the least amount of harm to the design of your document.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=130)** Don't get so focused on following these 33 laws that you lose sight of the goal, creating beautiful documents with gorgeous typography.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=141)** The famous typographer Adrian Frutiger said, "If you remember the shape of your spoon "at lunch, it has to be the wrong shape.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=151)** "The spoon and the letter are tools, "one to take food from the bowl, "the other to take information off the page.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=161)** "When it is a good design, the reader has "to feel comfortable because the letter "is both banal and beautiful."
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=171)** The 33 Laws of Typography will help ensure good type design in your documents.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=177)** People may not remember the typefaces you choose, and they may not remember all of the attention to detail that you put into your documents, but like Frutiger says, that's a good thing.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-33-laws-of-typography/goodbye?u=76281980&t=189)** Be happy knowing that readers will be comfortable in the banality and beauty of your typography.
